GOVERNOR BUDD SAYS
IT IS A DISGRACE
Denounces the Acts of Mayor Phelan's Demo
cratic Committee of One Hundred
Job Chasers.
The Alleged Convention Declared to Be Injurious to the
Interests of the Party in City and State.
The Legislative Programme.
There was a very warm session of
Judge Maguire's executive committee
yesterday afternoon in the rooms of the
Democratic State Central Committee in
the California Hotel. Governor Budd was
there. So was Mayor Phelan and several
of the lesser lights of the local and State
Democracy.
The heat of the session, which was held
behind closed doors, was due to Governor
Budds strictures on the manner in which
Mayor Phelan. "Whispering" Gavin Mc-
Nab and their select committee of one
hundred job-chasers are conducting the
affairs of the local Democracy.
The Governor denounced the action of
the Committee of One Hundred in resolv
ing Itself Into a nominating convention
as a shame and an outrage and an insult
to the Democratic voters of the City of
Ban Francisco. He further declared that
this action, which he characterized as in
famous, would result in the defeat of the
ticket in this city and would very ma
terially injure the chances of Maguire's
election. Ho said that it would hurt tue
party In the State as well as in San
Francisco, and | should not be counten
anced by honest men. He said much more
to the same effect, and Mayor Phelan,
Charles W. Fay and some others who
had been Instrumental in perfecting the
claims of the Committee of One Hundred
to the lives and liberties of local Demo
crats spent a very uncomfortable half
hour. 1 Incidentally, a very material part
of the programme of the self-constituted
convention was brought to light.
The | executive J committee met osten
sibly for the purpose of considering the
appeal made by Francis J. Heney from
the decision of the Committee of One
Hundred to resolve itself into a nominat
ing convention. The real purpose of the
meeting, however, was to devise some
echeme whereby Mr. Honey's protest
could be sidetracked and the voters of
the party be appeased and made to be-
Jieve that the members of the Commit
tee of One Hundred' are honorable men
Whose solo purpose in life is to promote
the welfare of the San Francisco Democ
â raer.~> r "Trw&s-rwnneraiscussing- the best
plan for putting Mr. Hene- and his ap
peal on the shelf that Governor Budd
gave vent to his disapproval of the high
handed methods that have up to the pres
ent time marked the proceedings of the
Committee of One Hundred.
Mayor Phrlan rushed to the defense of
his lieutenants, but even he could not
find it In his heart to wholly approve of
their action. He said that it was his
opinion that the Committee of One Hun
dred had not acted with "wise discretion"
In constituting Itself a nominating con
vention, but that the deed was now done
and it was the duty of every good Demo
crat to make the best Â«f it. He even
went so far as to declare that he was
surprised to find that the committee had
the power to constitute itself a nominat
ing body.

The Nameless Party.
The so-called People's party or fusion
organization is to have a day, the Su
preme Court having decided to give it
a chance to tell why it should have a
nnme and place in politics. When the
pinto combination applied to Secretary of
State Brown for baptism the State offi
cial refused to grant the favor, not be
ing satisfied with the paternity of the
applicant. Although realizing that it was
of very questionable origin the thought
of going through life nameless was too
rauch, and an application was made to
The San Francisco Call
It w*s at this point that Governor
Budd declared his conviction that the ac
tion of the committee of l>/0 and its sub
ordinates was an outrage and would work
an immediate and lasting Injury to the
party in city and State. xne Governor
had something else to say. He asked
Mr. Fay for some Information as to the
plan that had been adopted by the man
agers relative to the selection of legisla
tive nominees.
Fay replied that the plan was to au
thorize Chairman McGee to select a mem
ber of the convention from each Assem
bly District, such members, so selected
being authorized uf name the legislative
nominee from the district In which he
lived. It was apparent that this plan put
into the hands of Chairman McGee or
the men who controlled his acts the pow
er to name the whole Democratic legis
lative ticket. It was on this point the
Governor had a few words to say. He
declared that Chairman McGee has only
be*n a resident of San Francisco for a
period of seven months and has never
voted here; that the proposition to place
such a power in the hands of such a man
was simply suicidal if it was considered
as a bona fide act.
He declared that If McGee was given
this power without any strings to it that
he (McGee), not having a knowledge of
the needs of the city or of the qualifica
tions of the many aspirations for Legis
lative nominations, could not fulfill his
trust. If on the other hand McGee was
but the Instrument in the hands of the
men who dominated the convention the
condition was equally as bad, but for a
different reason.
"This man McGeee," said the Governor,
"is my personal and political enemy, and
is known to be such. It is also a matter
of common belief that I am a candidate
for election to the United States Senate.
Knowing these things you have put into
the hands of this man the power to vir
tually appoint Legislative nominees who
are prejudicial to my candidacy. I want
to state here and now that I am not and
will not bf a camildaA* for Â«MÂ«Â»ction to the
United States Senate â and there are no
strings on that declartlon like there
are on a declaration recently made by M.
H. de Toung.
"That, however, does not chnnge the
fact about what you propose to do. I
do not believe that the Democrats of San
Francisco will stand Â»uch a high-handed
proceeding as this, and I for one will not
blame them."
The Governor's criticism was not very
palatable to the Mayor and his friends,
but this fact did not deter the Governor
from giving vent to his feelings on the
subject. The net result of the meeting
was that Mr. Heney's protest and ap
peal were quietly placed on the shelf.
A meeting of the State Central Commit
tee will be called for some time during
the present month.

SAN FRANCISCO, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1-898.
MAYOR PHELAN IS
RENOMINATED BY
THE PROGRAMMERS
Democrats Fear Disruption, and After
Naming Four Judges Adjourn
Their Convention.
There is dissension among the members
of the Democratic committee of one
hundred now acting as a nominating
convention for the Democratic party of
San Francisco.
The initial .Session of the convention wag
held Monday evening, and last evening
another session was held at which busi
ness was transacted down to the point
where trouble threatens. When this
point was reached. Master of Ceremo
nies Charles W. Fay, who enjoys the rep
utation of being the mouthpiece of his
Honor, the Mayor, moved that the con
vention adjourn until to-morrow evening.
No one objected and it was so ordered.
Fay and his friends, who have in hand
the task of making up the slate, hope
that between now and Thursday even
ing they will be able to satisfy the de
mands of some of the members of the
convention who object to having two or
three of the nominations go to men who
are members of the committee of one
hundred. On the other hand, the ob
jectors declare that they Intend to have
all the patronage that Is at the disposal of
the convention or they will raise such a
row that Mayor Phelan and Whispering
Gavin McNab wll find that the work of
bossing the Democracy requires more
than the sanction of the trustees named
by the State Central Committee.
The net result of last night's session
of the convention was the renomination
of Mayor James D. Phelan and the nom
ination of four Superior Court Judges.
MEN WHO CAUGHT THE EIE OF THE CONVENTION.
Judge William T. Wallace, Judge James
M. Soawell, Judge Rhodes Borden and
Attorney Frank Morasky were the fortu
nate candidates for the Superior Bench
nomination. United States District At
torney Bert Schlosingcr and F. V. Cos
tcllo were the unsuccessful candidates.
As on the previous evening there was
a numerous lobby present and it took
such a lively interest in the proceedings
of the convention that at times it re
quired the efforts of several policemen
to keep things in. working order. Mem
bers of the lobby made frequent calls
for the "slate" and "programme," but
aa Chairman McGee declined to respond,
tltfe interested spectators were compelled
to confine their attention to oral criti
cism of members of the convention.
Although the convention was. called for
8 o'clock it was several minutes past 9
o'clock before Chairman McGee rapped
for order. The delay was due to an un
successful effort on the part of the pro
grammers to secure the unquestioning
support for the slate of the disgruntled
Job chasers. The rollcall disclosed the
presence of eighty-eight members of the
committee of one hundred.
The resignation of Edmund Godchaux
was read and accepted, after which the
secretary read a resolution declaring that
all of the labor at the disposal of the
convention and its nominees should be
given to the members of the Allied
Trades Council. The report of the commit
tee on platform was then called for. The
report was read by Joseph N. Nougues
and was accepted by the convention. The
platform, which is printed in full in
these columns, denounced boss rule In
politics, pledges the nominees of the con
vention to carry out all sorts of reforms,
besides approving of the result of the
late war with Spain. This having been
dipposed of Chairman McGee announced
that nominations for Mayor were in or
der.
Dr. R. Beverly Cole had been selected
as the person to place the name of Mayor
Phelan before the convention. He made
a short but a very appropriate address,
in which he eulogized the work done by
Mayor Phelan during his Incumbency as
the city's chief executive.
"I am an old man," he said, "but I am
still young enough to recognize true mer
it. The man whose name I desire to
place before you for your consideration
is one whom you all know and whom you
! respect and honor. Since his elevation to
the highest place in the gift of the peo
ple of this city his name has become a
household word. He is, besides, a native
son. Although a young man he has an
old head, and his acts have always been
such as would conserve the best inter
ests of the people of San Francisco. Since
his incumbency of the office he has been
the watchdog of the treasury (laughter)
and has saved the city untold thousands
of dollars. The man to whom I refer is
James G. Phelan. He asks that his every
act be seen and criticized."
' The mention of Mayor Phelan's name
provoked great applause from floor and
gallery and at the request of Max Pop
per three cheers were given. Jasper N.
McDonald, chairman of the committee of
one hundred, asked the privilege of sec
i onding the nomination of Mr. Phelan. He
! declared that he would make no speech
! and that his only desire was to subscribe
to all that Dr. Cole had said. Dr. Cole
then moved that the nominations be
closed, and on motion of Clitus Barbour
the secretary was instructed to cast the
ballot for Mr. Phelan.
The chair appointed Mr. McDonald and
Colonel Sullivan a committee to find and
escort the Mayor to the convention ball.
His appearance was greeted with an
other season of cheering and handclap
ping, during which he bowed his appre
ciation and smiled v s thanks. He said;
"I thank you sln^ erely for the nomina
tion you have just given me. lam not
unfamiliar with the history of this con
vention (laughter) and I desire to signify
my approval of the action of the gov
erning body of the Democracy of this
State in its effort to take the party In
this city out of the hands of corrupt
bosses.
"You. gentlemen of the convention, rep
resent in its best sense the Democratic
party of San Francisco. No honest man
can say that this convention does not
come from the highest authority of me
party in this State. It was brought into
existence in order that the people of
this city might be relieved of the rule of
corrupt bosses and that they might
have a voice in conducting tne affairs of
the party. The affairs of the party may
be trusted with the people because the
great mass of people is always right.
"Af^er your deliberations have been
concluded I am sure that you will pre
sent to the people of San Francisco a
ticket composed of good and honest men
whose election will insure an honest ad
ministration of the aiiairs of the munici
pality. For these reasons I feel grateful
at receiving at the hands of such a con
vention the nomination which you have
given me.
"I know that the Supervisors and
School Directors who were elected two
years ago have not done their duty, al
though the Democratic party pledged
them to reforms. While this is to be de
plored, It cannot now be helped, and it Is
your business to purge the ticket of the
men who have been false to their pledges
in the past. Ido not desire to engage in
a general condemnation of the men with
whom I have tried to work anu will not
do so at this time.
"You have, I understand, adopted a
good platform which, if carried out, will
rid this city of the abuses that have
marked the conduct of its government.
But the plntform alone will not do the
work. You must name men who are lion
estâmen who have the interest of the city
at heart, and who will have the courage
to carry out the reforms which you have
planned and the pledges which you will
exact. I thank you from the bottom of
n\v heart."
The first number, on the programme hav
ing been carried out without a hitch,
Chairman McGee declared the next busi
ness in ord.er was the naming of four Su
perior Court Judges. Before any nomina
tions could be made, however, Joseph
Notigues moved that each candidate
whose name was presented to the conven
tion be Â» - equested to make a contribution
to the convention of not less than $10, the
same to be paid in at the time the can
didates' name was presented. An excep
tion was made in the case of School Di
rectors. A. Comte Jr., who had been
slated to make several nominations, and
who saw that under this rule he would
be required to produce a very considerable
amount of money, entered a very vigorous
protest. He declared that such action was
a relic of barbarism and that In his opin
ion the members of the convention should
bear the expense of the convention. A
standing vote was called for and the mo
tion was carried by a two-thirds majority.
Barna McKinne took the platform for
the privilege of placing Bert Schleslngerin
nomination and had just begun his ora
tion when he was interrupted by a rude
request for the necessary |10 entrance fee.
Having "produced" he proceeded to
enumerate the virtues of his candidate.
He continued to speak for fifteen or
twenty minutes and concluded without
having named Mr. Schlesinger, an omis
sion of which he was reminded in a very
pointed manner by members of the lobby.
He was followed by A. Comte Jr., who
presented $10 and the name of Frank J.
Murasky to the convention. He declared
that not a member of the convention had
been solicited to vote for Murasky. Some
one in the lobby proposed three cheers
for the candidate and the outsiders had a
little noise-making of their own.
Emll Pohli next took the floor, handed
up a twenty-dollar note, received $10
change, and proposed the name of Judge
James M. Seawell.
The next speaker was W. B. Cope, who
had concealed about his person the. name
of Judge Rhodes Borden and a ten-dollar
note, both of which he produced between
Interruptions from the gallery. Judge E.
A. Brldgeford, having: lirst paid his fine,
presented the name of "a young- man
who possesses all of the Jeffersonian
qualifications." F. V. Costello.
A. Comte Jr. found another ten dollar
piece, and after separating himself from
it was permitted to place Judge William
T. Wallace in nomination. Jerome Deasy
wanted to second the nomination of
Judge Wallace, but cut his speech short
when some one in the lobby shouted
"That fellow owes me rent."
There being no further nominations the
convention proceeded to ballot. It was
observed that Francis J. Heney had re
fused to sit in the convention,, and when
his name was called he arose from his
place In the lobby and declined to vote.
The ballot resulted as follows: Borden
70,~Wallace 65, Seawell t>3, Murasky 03,
Schlesinger 55, Costello 43. Borden, Wal
lace, Seawell and Murasky having re
ceived the highest votes cast were de
clared the nominees of the convention.
This business having been disposed of
Bart Burke of Santa Cruz moved that in
future no nominating speech should ex
ceed five minutes In length. This having
been agreed to the convention was just
settling itself down in anticipation of the
expected row over the other nominations,
when Charles W. Fay bobbed up in his
seat and moved that the convention ad
journ until Thursday evening. No objec
tion was offered and the adjournment
was ordered. It is expected that some
exciting caucuses will be held between
now and the time when Chairman Me-
Gee raps for order to-morrow evening.

STILL IN THE FIELD
The Kinne-Gillespie Contingent Decided Last
Night to Hold a Local Nomi
nating Convention.
They Accuse the "Middle-of-the-Roadsters" of Shamelessly
Keeping in Close Communication With Reputed
Railroad Lobbyists.
The Magulre-Gallagher-Glllespie-Klnne
wing of the Populists met last night and

held on to the records. Dr. W. N. Gris

STORY OF SUFFERING
AND ILL-TREATMENT
The soldiers who arrived from Hono
lulu yesterday on the Australia, on Mr
lough, tell hard stories of their treat
ment, not only on board the transport
Lakme, but in camp near Honolulu.
Their grievances began with embark
ation. It was originally intended that
only 160 men should be taken, but when
the steamer sailed there were 340 pri
vates and fifteen officers on board. The
results, according to their accounts,
were that aside from lack of sleeping
accommodations they were not half
fed. Those who got nearest the pro-
vender first appropriated it all and the
unfortunate outsiders went hungry.
Complaints were of no avail. On one
occasion a box of crackers was spilled
on the deck and there was a regular
football scrimmage to secure them.
Bread, salt meat and coffee constituted
the bill of fare for six days, and there
after they subsisted on hardtack and
coffee.
Army regulations require 1000 cubic
feet of air space per soldier. On the
Lakme the quarters afforded sixty-five
feet per man, and to make matters
worse the hold was very damp.
Arrived at Honolulu conditions were
as bad or worse, according to- the corn-
plaining soldiers.
"There seems to have been plenty of

SLAYS TWO OFFICERS
BEFORE HE IS TAKEN
Murder Done by a Man Who Sought
to Deprive His Father of a
Home.
GARRETT, Pa., Sept. 20.â E. P. My
ers this morning took possession of a
house left to him by his mother, Mrs.
Christley Myers, who died five months
ago. The deed was not signed by his
father, who had a life tenure In the
property. When Christley Myers ar
rived this morning he found the house
barricaded, his son refusing him en
trance. He at once went to Somerset,
swore out a warrant for forcible entry
and detainer, and on his return here
sought service for the writ. Young
Myers fired at the approaching Consta
ble, fatally wounding Michael Kearney.
Theti he fled to his boarding house, im
mediately adjoining, whither Constable
Nelson Lenhart and his brother, John
Lenhart, followed him. He fired again,
killing John Lenhart instantly.
â¢Sheriff Martin Hartzell of Somerset
in a short time arrived with a posse.
A crowd of infuriated citizens was
guarding the boarding house, in the
cellar of which young Myers was hid-
ing.
After an hour's waiting a raid was
made. A dozen shots were exchanged.
Myers was finally overpowered and
while being taken down the steps of the
house was struck by a gun in the
hands of somebody in the crowd. He
was knocked unconscious, but later re
covered and was removed to Somerset.
Michael Kearney died about 10
o'clock. John Lenhart, the other vic
tim, was scarcely recognizable when
found, the entire front of his face hav
ing been blown off.

New Charter.
During tho last two years the reform ele
ment at the Democratic party united ivlth
all good citizens In resisting the attempt
made by the allied political bosses of all
parties and the corporations enjoying ex
clusive ' privileges in electing a board of
fifteen freeholders and subsequently In
adopting the charter which that body
drafted. We now, for the consummation of
tUÂ«' patriotic campaign, n-sulting in the
adaption of the charter, call upon the peo
ple of the State to require of their legisla
tive representatives to record and dispute
the voice of the people of this city unmis
takably spoken, and we pledge In our own
behalf our legislative nominees to vote for
the ratification of the charter.
Gas and Electric Light.
We favor the adequate illumination of the
city, Riving light to the suburbs and bril
liancy to the streets, and pledge our nom
inees for Supervisors to give every facility
to corporations supplying light to compete
for the business of the city and of the citi
zens. The Democratic party Is, opposed to
granting special privileges which Inevit
ably result In monopoly, and no long as
light Is supplied by private corporations
there should be nn discrimination in favor
of one as against the other. The rates
charged are notoriously excessive, and we
pledge our nominees to a substantial re
duction based upon the value of their In
vestment, as indicated by reference In this
platform to the water question.
Wt- pledge our nominees for Supervisors
to require, within a district to be deter
mined by thorn, the wires of all corporations
to be laid under ground after reasonable
notice.
Streets and Sewers.
We pledge our nominees for Supervisors
and Superintendent of Streets to improve
the streets, to maintain and keep them
clean when they are Improved, and to pre
pare specifications under which street work
may be done that will not exclude any
meritorious pavement.
We demand that the sanitary condition
of the city be carÂ»fully studied and that
the severs be flushed and otherwise kept
clean. We" recommend the adoption of
some gystetn by which salt water may be
used for, cleansing purposes. We demand
that all, unused rails and tracks and un
necessary turn-tablen artd such other ob
structions ;be J-emovÂ«d from ths streets.
Health.
The preservation of the health of the
people is the first duty of municipal gov
ernment and we commend the efforts of
the Board of Health to destroy tho deadly
traffic In adulterated goods, and we pledge
support to their crusade until our citizens
can have reasonable guarantees that pois
onous and deleterious foods are excluded
from our market.

Schools.
We pledge our nominees for members of
the Board of Education to segregate each
year a sufficient Bum for the purpose of
paying salaries of the teachers In full bo
the same shall be paid monthly and to
sacredly set apart such . appropriation for
the purpose for which It Is made. This
done we pledge our nominees for School
Directors to provide ample accommodation
for the pupils of the city, and artistic and
sanitary school houses. We further pledge
our nominees for members of the Board
of Education to elect no teacher during
their term of ' office unless vacancies are
created by death, resignation or otherwise,
because we realize the fact that the staff
of teachers employed, now by the city and
county Is sufficient to meet all possible de
mands upon the schools for the ensuing
year. In the spirit of civil service reform
we pledge our nominees also to promote to
the higher positions In the department only
teachers who have by long and meritorious
service shown their peculiar fitness.
Franchises.
In granting franchises we pledge our
nominees for Mayor and Supervisors to
stipulate conditions for white labor and
California material and the free transpor
tation, while on official duty, of members
of the police, fire and health departments
and postmen in the employ of the Gov
ernment. We further pledge our nominees
to vote /or the renewal of no franchise at
a date earlier than one year before the
expiration of the original' franchise.
Labor.
In all .contracts let by the Board of
Supervisors and other boards and on all
public work we pledge our nominees for
all offices having discretion In such mat
ters tp stipulate for a daily wage of not
less than $2 for eight hours' work. ""
Chinese Labor.
The Chinese question is again pressing
Itself upon this community for solution.
The Government of the United States has
permitted the uniforms of the brave de
fenders of, the flag to be made In Chinese
sweat shops by servile labor. This scan
dalous and distressing fact has called the
attention of the public to the condition of
people of our own race engaged In similar
employments, and It has been made known
by the Labor Commissioner of the State of
California. Mr. E. L. Fitzgerald, that much
of the poverty and distress In this city Is
due to the crowding out of men and women
from employment by Chinese cheap labor.
The Chinese have Invaded the business dis
trict with stores In which they exhibit all
kinds of articles of clothing. The Chinese
employed â In :. the manufacture .dt these
goods. In deadly and disastrous competi
tion with our own people, observe no hours
of labor, have no families to rear and edn- "
cate, have practically no taxes to pay. have
no social or other organizations to support,
and in no manner contribute to the social
well being" of the community In time of
peace or its defense in time of \Â»arÂ» Their
earnings, with their bones, are sent to
China, and the distribution of the wealth
which should secure prosperity for the com
munity is wasted in their hands. They are
not patrons of our merchants, and their
cheap labor, on account of their non-par
ticipation In the ordinary commercial and '
social affairs of life, is a blight to our
community. We pledge our nominees for all
public offices In which the Interests of labor
may be guarded to grant some remedial
legislation. We demand that some law be
passed, and so pledge our nominees. 1 - by ;
which the people can Identify by mark the.
goods made in Chinese sweat shops, so that
our patriotic people may be enabled to dis
criminate between the labor of their own
kind and that of those who live like para
sites upon the community and. In an ecoÂ»
comic sense, drain it of its, substance. V~=~.i>
| Home Industries.
Â»We call on all citizens to purchase no <
other goods than those of home production,
and manufacture, and thereby revive our
industries and employ our people.
Beligious Freedom.
The convention deplores the introduction
Into our political and social life of religious
tests, born of bigotry and intolerance, and
emphatically condemns any efforts to
thwart the liberal provisions of the consti
tution of our country with respect to abso
lute religious freedom.
City Officers.
We pledge our nomlnes for County Clerk
to conduct his office at a cost not to exceed
$76,000 per annum. Including his own salary,
and call the attention of the people of the
city and State to the outrage perpetrated
upon the Republican system of government
itself by the nomination of C. F. Curry, a
confessed pledge breaker and present in
cumbent of this office, for Secretary of
State. We pledge our nominees for Super
visors and Surveyor to reduce at least 33 1-3
per cent the fees now charged by the City
and County Surveyor, and tn allow no
charge In except of the Fchedul* now estab
lished by the Society of Civil Engineers.
We pledge our nominee for Recorder to
pay not more than 10 cents per folio for
copying inFtruments.
We pledge our nominee for Sheriff to turn
all fees Into the city and county treasury.
We pledge our nominee for District At
torney to prosecute with vigor all male
factors, especially In official places.
The office of Public Administrator is a
trust which has heretofore conferred enor
mous emoluments on the Incumbent and hla
attorney without any advantage to the
county treasury. If a public office be a pub
lic trust, and not a private "snap or
special privilege, then we pronounce tha
present system of converting an official
station Into a personal benefaction as con
trary to Democratic principles, and recom
mend Its rectification by appropriate legis
lation, and we particularly approve of the
proposed enactment that a Public Adminis
trator thall not employ counsel at the ex
pense of any f-statÂ» without the approval
of the court In advance of such employ
ment.
We pledgpe our legislative nominees to
favor legislation looking to the reduction of
the cost of litigation, especially In probate
tribunals, and the cutting down of the com
missions of executors and administrators,
and by a maximum schedule, attorneys'
fees, us proposed In the Braunhart bill, pre
sented to the last legislature.
We pledge our nominees for Superior and
Police Judges to put the shorthand reporters
of their courts upon a salary basis not ex
ceeding $200 per month. Inclusive of all work
to be performed by them, and to charge not
more than 5 cents per folio for transcrib
ing.
We condemn and call upon the people to
disapprove constitutional amendment No 1
providing for the payment of the city's
debts, because through the machinations of
certain street contractors, whose claims
have long since been rejected and outlawed,
the door of the treasury Is thrown wide open
to the payment of upward of $5,000,000.
We pledge our nominees for city and
county offices to give way to the provisions
of the new charter, which calls for a gen
eral municipal election In November, 1899,
so that that Important instrument may be
inaugurated according to its own terms as
approved by the people.
The nominees must sign this platform
within ten days from the date of its adop
tion, failing in which we declare their
places lpso facto vacated, and direct and
empower the committee on vacancies of this
convention to make new nominations for
the places so vacated.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1898.
DEMOCRATS
HOLD THEIR
PRIMARIES
Will Probably Fuse
With Populists.
CONVENTIONS SAME DAY
AN INDEPENDENT TICKET MAY
BE BORN.
Unfortunate Candidates Are Busy
Telling Each Other How They
Failed of Nomina
tion.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
90S Broadway, Sept. 20.
Next Saturday the Democrats will hold
their county convention. There is not
a rush of candidates for the offices, but
still there are some who are willing to
take their chances. This evening the
primaries were held all over the county,
and the convention will be composed of
158 delegates.
The Populists primaries were also held
this evening, and their convention will
take place in Grand Army Hall next Sat
urday. An attempt is now being made
to appoint committees from each of the
conventions to agree upon a basis by
which the nominations can be divided
and a. complete fusion ticket nominated.
The Populists are split right in two, the
middle-of-the-road men having already
held their convention, and nominated a
county ticket.
Now that the Republican convention Is
over there is much talk of putting up a
complete Independent ticket. Already
enough candidates have signified their
intention of making independent cam
paigns to form the nucleus of such a
ticket.
James Taylor is already running inde-
Eendently for Coroner, Louis Schaffer for
heriff, A. K. Grim for Recorder, and it
is now said that Justice Clift could be
persuaded to run for District Attorney;
John Cronin for Township Justice, and
E. L. Lawrence for Justice of the Peace
of Brooklyn Township. Others are also
mentioned who would be willing to make
up a complete independent ticket, but
no such ticket would cut any figure In
this party year.
Justice Lawrence is the most surprised
man In the county to-day. He went to
the convention yesterday in a brand new
suit of clothes, In one pocket of which
was the nomination to succeeed himself.
Somehow or other, by one of those quirks
so common in untrammeled conventions.
Professor L. V. Inskeep received the
nomination, and Justice Lawrence is still
trying to llgure out how it was done.
Constable Cronin thinks he was not
rightly treated by the district conven
tion of Oakland Township. He also had
a cinch until the evening before the con
vention, but in the struggle Justice
Quinn came out on top. Cronin said that
Colonel Dan Burns turned him down, at
which everybody laughed, for this is the
first intimation that Colonel Burns took
any interest in the county ticket, al
though It is not impossible that he had an
interest in the legislative ticket.
The new Republican County Central
Committee elected yesterday Is as fol
lows: First Ward. W. \V. Morrison; Sec
ond Ward, A. H. KreÂ«d: Third Ward,
George E. Randolph- Fourth Ward, R.
C. Potts; Frfth Ward, Clarence Cro won ;
Sixth Ward, Frank Barnett; Seventh
Ward. W. B. Pringle: Alameda, J. B.
Lanktree; Brooklyn, J. H. W. Riley;
Murray, J. O. McKown; AVashington, S.
C. Archibald; Eden, 11. D. Brunner; Oak
land Township, J. A. B. Hutton.

A Hard Fight Against Freese.
A hard fight is being made against Cap
tain A. C. Freese, who Is seeking renomi
natlon for Public Administrator at the
hands of the local Democracy. After
looking around for several weefcjs for an
available man, the opponents of Freese
in the Committee of One Hundred decided
to back Dr. Washington Dodge, at present
a member of the Board of Supervisors,
for the places The result is that there is
a lively row on hand. Dr. Dodge, while
willing to accept the nomination for Pub
lic Administrator, is looking with envious
eye on the Assessorship, and it is said
that if the least opportunity affords itself
he will drop his political managers and
make a run for tne office held at pres
ent by John Siebe. Some of his friends
are now beginning to strenuously object
to his flirting with two offices filled with
golden political plums.
The Republicans would not weep over
the turning down of Freese, for they rec
ognize in him a strong candidate for any
office for which he may be nominated.

HOISTED IN HAVANA
It Is Raised Over the Headquarters
of the Evacuation Com
mission.
HAVANA, Sept. 20.â At 10 o'clock
i this morning the first American flag
! was hoisted on the flagstaff of the Tro
| cha Hotel, the headquarters of the
i Evacuation Commission. A guard of
j marines was posted at the entrance to
i the grounds.
Colonel Cloud and Captain Payne
I stood on the roof of the hotel at either
I side'of the flagstaff. At 10 r/clock sharp
I a quartermaster of the steamer Reso
lute hoisted the flag, which unfurled
! and fluttered proudly in the breeze.
All the members of the commission
raised their hats and cheered the Stars
i and Stripes. The approaches to the
I ground at either end of the street were
i patrolled by squads of the Guardia
Civile in order that the commissioners
| might not be molested.
This morning a widow and two chil
dren, evidently persons of refinement,
! but In abject want, approached General
I Wade, the president of the commission,
j and pleaded for relief. The widow -and
i children were seated In the dining
room of the hotel and given a hearty
! meal. Others in need were equally
j well treated, all who applied receiving
I relief of some soft.

DEATH ENDS THEIB FEUD.
Duel on the Mississippi in Which
Both Principals Are Slain.
ST. LOUIS, Sept. 20.--Word was re
ceived here to-day of a fatal duel be
tween Mate Bart Pavis and Night Watch
man Charles Baker on board the steamer
Mayflower of the Diamond Jo line, which
left here last night for Memphis. Both
men are deud.
For some time there had been ill feel
ing between the two men, and last night,
when the boat was tied up at Hugs
Landing, one mile from Croston, the
watchman attacked the mate with a
knife. For some time the struggle lasted,
both participants rolling about the deck
to the horror of the passengers, who were
unable to interfere. Finally, when the
mate, who had been cut in numerous
places, was about to give up, he suc
ceeded in drawing his revolver, and, with
a well directed shot, he laid ihe watch
man dead.
Stephen Hugs, proprietor of the land
ing, went up town for an officer and re
turned with Deputy Coroner Bell. They
found both the mate and watchman dead,
lying on the bloody deck.

County Tax Levies,

included in the rate fixed. The rate Is

1.58 1-3 outside.

est tax rate for many years.

A New Choral Society.
A choral society of both sexes haÂ« re
cently been organized for the study of
English part-songs, glees and madrigals,
with the ultimate intention of producing
some good comic opera. The rehearsals
take place every Wednesday evening at
8 o'clock in the basement of the Church
of the Advent, Eleventh street, near
Market, which has been secured on ac
count of ' its accessibility, it being ad
jacent to all the leading car lines of the
city. The society Is a purely secular one,
having no connection with any church,
and the public Is cordlaly Invited to join.

ENDEAVORING TO FfX
OAKLAND'S TAX LEVY
THE COUNCIL STILL UNDECIDED
AS TO THE RATE.
It Will Probably Be $1 36âProvis
ion Is Made for Improving
Lake Merrltt.
OAKLAND, Cal., Sept. 20.â At the meet-
Ing of the City Council to-night Coun
cilman Cuvelier introduced an ordinance
providing' a tax levy of $1 86. In speaking
about the measure he said that he had
provided a tax of $1 for the running ex
penses of the city government and for
Some sewer extensions, having practically
accepted the estimates of the Auditor on
the former. The only difference he made
was to cut the street light and the street
department funds a little, and not to
leave quite so much money in the general
fund.
As to the funds for the improvement of
Lake Merrltt and the building of sewers
adjacent, Mr. Cuvelller said that he had
provided for the raising of the entire
amount this fiscal year, as was provided
in the ordinance already passed to print.
In explanation of this feature of his ordi
nance, Mr. Cuvellier said he did not be
lieve in "taking two bites at a cherry?"
He stated that he had talked with vari
ous business men, and they said to go
ahead and make improvements; they
would not object to a few cents increase
In the tax levy. Mr. Cuvelller asserted
that the increase of the rate over $1 21
would not cost the man who paid taxes
on $1000 over $1 50. lie had also provided
for the extension of the east side sewer
and the Cemetery Creek Intercepting
sewer In his ordinance.
Councilman Girard also introduced an
ordinance fixing the tax levy at $1 15
Mr. Girard cuts off half of the Lake Mer
ritt appropriation and also the two sew
ers near the lake, but adds to the street
fund. Both ordinances were referred to
the committee with the suggestion that
when the original ordinance comes up
next Monday any changes may be made
by amendment in the regular manner. '
McKINLEY MAY COME WEST.
The President Expressed a Desire to
Visit the Pacific Coast.
Colonel Henry J. Kowalsky, who has
lately returned from the East, is of the
opinion that President McKlnley may
very easily be persuaded to visit Califor
nia after his trip to the Omaha Exposi
tion. Colonel Kowalsky was in Washing
ton about three weeks ago, and at. that
time had a personal interview with the
President on the subject. "President
McKinley expressed to me a very great
desire to visit California," said Colonel
Kowalsky. "He hns always been inter
ested in the State and has relatives here,
and told me that he had always looked
forward to a trip to the Pacific Coast.
He said if he could possibly arrange
it he would endeavor to make the trip
after visiting the Peace Jubilee at
Omaha and If so would spend several
weeks In the State.
"I believe that an urgent invitation
from the people of the State, and es
pecially from San Francisco, would bring
President McKinley to the coast at this
time. He Is coming to Omaha, anyway,
and if we make the invitation strong
enough he will be very likely to favor us
with a visit."

FOOLED WITH A BEVOLVEB.
An Accident on Bernal Heights That
May Besult in a Boy's Death.
The danger and gross carelessness of
parents leaving weapons wrthin the reach
of children was exemplified in a case
Monday afternoon which may result in
the death of a boy.
Daniel Mulcahey. the four-year-old son
of Thomas Mulcahey of 13S Ripley street,
and his elder brother, 6 years of age, went
to the house of a neighbor, John Bur
nett to keep Burnett's son, a boy 7 years
of age, company while his mother was
downtown. , ,
The boys discovered a revolver, ana
thought it would be good fun to act as
soldiers. They got tired of drilling and
then it was suggested to see the smoke
coming from the muzzle of the revolver.
Burnett after some difficulty pulled the
trigger. There was a report and a flash
and Daniel Mulcahey with a yell dropped
to the floor. The other two boys thor
oughly frightened, ran to Mrs. Mulcahey
and told her -'hat had happened. She
procured assistance and the wounded boy
was carried to her home. . ;
Dr Rinqe was summoned and he round
that the bullet had struck the boy on the
left arm and gone through, splintering
the bone, came out at the arm pit, en
tered <he chest and gracing a lung
finally lodged in the back. The doctor
extracted the bullet, but he has grave
fears for the recovery of the boy.
Mrs. Burnett did not know the revolver
was loaded. It belonged to her husband,
who is employed on the steamer Isortn
Fork.
A HIGH-PBICED PLAYEB.
Harry Cor son Clarke Objected to Act
ing to "Popular" Prices.
Harry Corson Clarke, the star in the
bill at the New Comedy Theater, and
Manager Leavltt do not speak as they
pass. Clarke claims that the manager of
the house reduced the prices for seats
In the house contrary to the understand
ing: that was reached when the actor con
sented to play at the New Comedy, and
consequently that scale of prices com
monly known as "popular" tended to
cheapen him In the theatrical world.
Clarke and his manager, S. B. Rlcaby,
say that when they booked to play at the
theater the choice seats of the house were
to be 75 cents and $1. On Monday night,
when the show opened, the actor was as
tonished to find that the seats or the
whole lower floor were selling at 50 cents.
A complaint was made and Leavitt gave
promises to remedy the cause of the
actor's objections. Last night the pro
grammes again announced that seats
would be obtainable at "popular" prices
and tickets were sole at the rate Clarke
had found fault with.
The actor refused to go on with the play
unless something more than assurances
were given him that the high scale of
prices would prevail during the remain
der of his engagement. To-night the
seats will be 75 cents and SI, and Clarke
maintains that even if there will not be
as large a house to greet him his repu
tation as a first-class and high-priced
player will not cuffer.

SAN FRANCISCO'S WEALTH.
Auditor Broderick yesterday sent to
Controller Colgan at Sacramento his
annual report on the financial condition
of the city and county of San Francisco.
The exhibit is as follows: Total value
of real estate, $189,448,545; value of im
provements thereon, $93,321,185; value of
personal property, including money and
solvent credits, $69,574,331; total assessed I
valuation, $352,344,061. The assessed value |
of railroads (Southern Pacific) In the city
and county is $189,454. The total value o't
mortgages assessed is $R1, 240,500. The
amount of bonds outstanding is $291,000,
while the city cash on hand to redeem
the same is $195,543,362, leaving a net debt
of $95,456 38. The valuation put on all
parks, public school buildings, engine
houses and other city and county prop
erty is $25,000,257.

Chief Lees Sued.
Chief I. W. Lees was awakened from
his dream of power yesterday and
brought to a realization of things ter
restrial by the filing of a suit against him
for the recovery of money taken from J.
Ptcetti. James A. Devoto. as assignee of
the latter, filed the suit against the Chief
to recover $412, which, he alleges, was
taken forcibly from Picettl on August 20,
and which is now unlawfully retained.
The complaint also asks for $100 damages.
James C. Singer's Will.
The will of James C. Singer, who died
in this city last Monday after a debauch,
while on a visit from Butte, Mont., was
filed for probate yesterday. A petition
of the Public Administrator for special
letters u^on the estate of the deceased
was also filed. It recites Singer's owner
ship of stocks and bonds, cash and other
property, found on the person of the de
ceased. The property is bequeathed to
Mrs. Agnes Jenkins of Branchton, On
tario, and Mrs. Marion Bennie of De
troit, Mich.

CONFESSION IS
DISCREDITED
Claims That the Guilt of Dreyfus Was Morally
Though Not Legally Proved Before Evidence
Was Manufactured.
Now Believed That He Was Taking Money From Both Sides for
Furnishing Information Supposed to Be of a Highly
Treasonable Character.
CHICAGO, Sept. 20.â A London cable
to the Inter Ocean says: Major Count
Esterhazy, who was recently retired
from the French army, still remains in
Ijondon, where he is now in the hands
of a well-known firm of police court
lawyers. The latter issued a statement
!n his behalf to-day, declaring he has
not authorized any confession or reve
lations in v the Dreyfus case. He de
clared that he intends to act "for the
best interests of his country and her
army." This grandiloquent declaration
from such a source is so grotesque that
It Is amusing.
Esterhazy has told three persons and
probably more within a month that he
Wrote the Dreyfus borderau. He also
described an interesting list of other
crimes committed In connection with
the case by himself and others. He af
firms there was moral, not legal, proof
of Dreyfus' guilt, and that the officers
of the French general staff resorted to
manufactured evidence In order to se
cure his condemnation, which was
really deserved.
He declared he wrote the borderau In
obedience to orders from a superior of
ficer and justifies his action by reason,
already cabled, that a "soldier should
place his conscience as well as his
sword at the disposal of his superior
officers." But his story of how he came
to write the borderau is almost certain
ly false.
Pie wrote it and handed it to two mil
itary officers of foreign powers before
Dreyfus was dreamed of in connection
ivlth th. affair. He wrote it under in
structions from officers of the intelli
gence department, by which he was
employed, in order to obtain greater se
crets in return.
He was. however, playing a double
game. He was needy, his French
stipend being insufficient to meet his
expenses and he really received money
from both sides and actually delivered
BOLINAS' BOY BANDIT
REGRETS HIS FOLLY!
Realizes Too Late the Disgrace He i
Has Brought Upon His
Family.
SAN RAFAEL, Sept. . 20.â Victor Col
well, the youthful bandit who held up thÂ«
stage "running between this city and Bo-
Unas, Bat on the couch in his cell to
day the picture of abject misery and
despair. At the time of committing the
crime he .was partly under the Influence
of liquor, but now his mind has cleared
and he recognizes to the utmost his woe
ful predicament.
"I wish you would go and speak to my
Bister." he said to The Call representa
tive, "for God knows I cannot bear the !
ordeal. This must be an awful blow to i
my family."
Never did anybody speak more truly.
For months Grant Col well, the elder ':
brother, has been away from home, fight- :
Ing In his country's name as a member of
Roosevelt's Rough Riders, and during all
this time the anxious hearts at home j
have been living in momentary dread of I
hearing news of his fall under lire. Now
that the war is over and Grant is ex
pected home in a short time comes this
crushing blow, added to the disgrace
being the fear that the petted boy of the
family may have to spend a long term
behind prison walls.
When Miss Idelle Colwell, the prisoner's
only sister, heard the news, she was so
overcome by the shock that she had to
be assisted home. The poor mother is
very ill with grief.
Colwell himself is so despondent that :
It is feared he may attempt to commit ;
suicide, and the utmost precaution has |
been taken by Sheriff Harrison to pre- i
vent such an act. Every instrument by
which he could accomplish the act is !
kept out of his way, and his cellmate,
a man named Allen, has been requested I
to keep a strict watch on all Colwell's
movements. __ Â§>

AN ESCAPED CONVICT
MET BY A HUNTER
Alton H. Gould, a San Quentin
Trusty, Seen Near San
Rafael.
SAN RAFAEL. Sept. 20.â While hunting
on Jorgensen Hill this evening, be
tween this city and San Anselmo, a
youth named Angelo Albini met a man
exactly answering the description of Al
ton H. Gould, the convict that escaped
from San Ouentin Prison last Sunday
evening. On perceiving Albini the man
turned and ran out of sight. It was but
a short time until a number of guards
were on the trail, but up to a late hour
their quest was in vain.
At 5 p. m. yesterday Gould was seen in
the hills near West End Station by Wal
ter Hmith. He was men dressed in con
vict garb, but to-day he had on an old
coat and vest of dark material and a
lair of faded blue overalls, evidently
Etolf-n from some farm house.
The knowledge' that the misty is still at
large jias had the effect of keeping wo
men living in the outskirts of this clty
and in the county, between here and
Sausallto, in a state of nervous apprehen
sion. No children are permitted to play
In the streets, and the suburbs appear de
serted at night.

LOST IN THE MOUNTAINS.
Thrilling Experience of Mrs. Cyrus
Newton of Woodland.
WOODLAND, Sept. 20.â Cyrus Newton
and family have returned from a camping
trip in Mendocino County. During thHr
?xuy they had an experience which
neither Mr. Newton nor his wife will soon
forget. Mr. Newton left camp for a hunt.
He was gone so much longer than was
expected that Mrs. Newton feared Homc
thfng had happened to him and, takine
her 3-year-old child in her arms, started
in search of him. She wandered about
ueveral hours and finally lost her way.
Night coming on 'she was compelled to
remain out until morning. At daylight
the documents- named In the borderau.
When the fact that the documents
were missing was discovered Esterhazy
claimed the borderau from those to
whom it had been delivered. That was
why the borderau was in pieces and
not because it had been extracted from
the waste paper basket at the German
embassy.
The borderau was handed to a civil
spy, who was charged with its trans
mission to General Mercier, then Min
ister of War. As yet Dreyfus was posi
tively unsuspected. Esterhazy trusted
to a chapter of accidents for finding a
resemblance between his handwriting
and that of some one else, no matter
who.
Had the handwriting of the borderau
resembled that of any officer but a Jew,
and a Jew so thoroughly disliked as
was Dreyfus, the victim of the error
would have got off with five or ten
years' imprisonment.
But it was almost the counterpart of
the handwriting of the bete noir of the
general staff, for Dreyfus was that.
Given half a dozen Paty dv Clams,
Henrys, Sandherrs, who were without
scruples a-s to the means of satisfying
their hatred, and the rest followed as a
matter of course.
The manufacture of false evidence
did not end with the borderau, and
Esterhazy, could, if he chose, make
many more startling revelations, but he
is so utterly discredited, and so per
sistently mixes truth and falsehood
that it is doubtful if any statement he
makes will gain credence even among
the friends of Dreyfus, whose cause it
would help.
Esterhazy is now almost penniless.
He is extremely bitter against his
farmer associates, who have now aban
doned him. There are some indications
that the partial revelations of the past
few days will bring some of those whom
Esterhazy would involve by a full con
fession once more to his support. In
that case this precious individual will
probably become once more the cham
pion of the military honor and glory of
France.

her way back to camp. Footsore an<l
famished she had . almost abandoned
hope, when about 11 oVlock she sighted
a cabin. Here she obtained rest and|foo'J
and later was found by her husband, who,
on discovering her absence from camp,
walked ten miles for help and organizes
a searching party.
CORBETT AND M'COY '
MAY FIGHT AT ROBY
CLEVELAND. Sept. 20.â A special to
the Plain Dealer from La Porte, Ind..
says: Roby, which in past years was the
scene of many pugilistic battles, may be
made the arena for the pulling off of the
tight between Corbett and McCoy.
Private advices from Roby give the de
tails of the proposed meeting. Architects
have been at work for several days on the
plans for the conversion of the betting
ring at Roby into a fighting arena, with
a seating capacity of 10,000.
The details of the meeting*of the glad
iators are in the hands of Stuart, Consi
dine and John Condon of racetrack fame
and a secret meeting held at Mount Clem
ens. Mich., resulted in Roby being select
ed as the battleground for the two heavy
weights.
It is the evident purpose to keep both
McCoy and Corbett in training without
revealing the exact location of the fight.

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1898.
DEATH BY
CREMATION
FOR EIGHT
Great Loss of Life in
a Burning Grain
Elevator. .
Spontaneous Combustion of
Dust Causes Destruction
of the Building.
Workmen Perish Miserably or Gain
the Open Air Horribly Burned
or Crushed by the Fall-
Ing Kails,
Special Correspondence of The Call.
TOLEDO, 0., Sept. 20.â Eight persons
cremated and eight seriously injured is
the result of the most disastrous flre
that ever occurred in Toledo. The dead
are:
SAMUEL ALEXANDER.
BERT WAINWRIGHT.
FRED GARRET.
HAROLD PARKS.
JOHN SMITH.
GRACE PARKS.
FRANK VAN HOUSEN.
JOHN CARR.
The injured are:
Dave D. Kemp.
Barney Welch.'
Charles Keifer.
Fred Pardigillis.
Charles Brookseeker.
Ed Elliott.
Everett Smith. ,
Hamilton Parks.
William J. Parks,
W. C. Jordan.
Peter Haas.
Al Baldie.
Four others, names unknown.
The spontaneous combustion of duet
in the grain elevator owned by Pad
dock, Hodge & Co. at 9 o'clock caused
this destruction of life, and none of
those who were taken out after the
fire started were far enough away from
death's door to tell any of the details.
William J. Parks, the superintendent,
after having been blown through the
window of the lower story, was con
scious for a moment and said that at
about 8:30 a terrible explosion occurred
on the south side of the elevator. He
knew there were about twenty men at
work on the seven floors of the build
ing.
Besides those regularly employed at
the elevator, the three children of Su
perintendent Parks were visiting him
at the time. One of these may re
cover, but Grace, a 17-year-old girl, is
burned almost beyond recognition, and
Harold, the third child, has not been
found, having been either blown to
atoms or cremated.
All over Toledo houses were shaken
by the explosion as by an earthquake
and windows were shattered for blocks
around. Very soon afterward flames
burst from all sides of the elevator.
It was but a few minutes before the
Fire Department began the work of
rescue, which was rendered difficult by
the terrific heat. The river cut off es
cape on one side and there the flames
seemed to be less fierce. The families
of a dozen men who were known to
have been at work within rushed to
the scene and women calling for their
Imprisoned husbands, brothers and
fathers made a scene indescribable.
It was learned that a force of twenty
men had been In the building, their
purpose having been to load 80,000
bushels of grain during the night. No
one of the entire number could be seen
in any part of the building, and it was
impossible to reach them in any way.
William Parks was found first. He
was twenty feet from the building,
frightfully burned and his clothes al
most entirely torn off. He had been
hurled from his place in the main room
through a window and his cries were
most pitiful. Another employe, John
Carr, was hurled from the fifth floor of
the building and was found bleeding
and burned, with many bones broken.
He did not long survive. Fireman Da
vid Kemp and Charles Kelfer, the en
gineer, were found at their places in
the engine room. They were injured by
falling timbers/and their faces were
charred to a cnjsp by the flames.
The little daughter of William Parks
was sitting at the desk in the office at
the time of the explosion, and was
hurled out of the door. She walked
down the elevation on which the build
ing stands and fell, to be carried away
unconscious, suffering from injuries
from which she cannot recover.
John Smith was fatally burned. He
was literally disemboweled and was ta
ken to the hospital to die.
The missing men are doubtless all
dead. No trace can be found of any of
them and as they were employed at the
top of the elevator their chances for
escape were but slight.
The heat became so intense that
twenty cars standing on the siding near
the building were added to the loss.
The fire department had a hard strug
gle to save other elevators and prop-
erty.
The bridge across the Maumee River
was on fire several times, but outside
of the loss of the elevator the damage
is slight.
Mr. Paddock, a member of the firm
owning the plant, said there was be
tween 500,000 and 800,000 bushels of
grain In storage at the time, most of
it being winter wheat. The property
and the grain Is an entire loss and will
reach $450,000. The insurance is $135,000
on the building and the grain is covered
with $258,000 insurance.
At 12:20 o'clock the flames were under
control.
A later revision of the fire loss shows
the buildings and contents are valued
at $550,000. The damage to adjoining
property is $8000.
Superintendent Parks and one of the
unknown men died early this morning
from their injuries, making the total
number of fatalities ten.
EVACUATION OF
PORTO RICO NOW
WELL UNDER WAY
EQtire Island Will Be Turrjed Over
to the flmericaQS Within
Three WeeKs.
Special Cable to The Call and the Hew York Herald. Copyrighted, 1898, ,
by James Gordon Bennett.
SAN JUAN, Porto Rico, Sept. 20.â Evacuation was begun In ear
nest to-day. Four hundred Spanish officers and soldiers embarked on
the steamer San Francisco, bound for Spain. The detachment march
ed quietly to the wharf and entered barges, which wer â¢ towed to the
ship. A curious crowd was attracted, but there was no demonstra
tion.
Twelve hundred more Spaniards will sail to-morrow, and two
large transports are expected to arrive September 26, which will
carry the remainder.
The Spanish Commissioners announced at to-day's meting that
they expected to turn the island over to the American authorities with
in three weeks. It was agreed to-day to immediately evacuate five
towns at the eastern end of the island. The concentration of the
movement to San Juan has now fully begun. Cayey and Caguas will
probably be evacuated next week.
SPAIN NOT DISPOSED
TO YltLO EVERYTHING
Will Seek to Compromise in the Ne
gotiations to Be Conducted at
Paris.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â A Sun cable
from Madrid says: The Cabinet council
was in session five hours to-day. The
Ministers fully approved the instructions
to the peace commissioners, who will
start for Paris on Sunday.
The instructions to the Spanish peace
commissioners covered several pages and
are mainly devoted to the question of the
Philippines. They are less precise re
specting the West Indies, the disposition
of which was virtually settled by the pro
tocol.
Nevertheless, the commissioners are
definitely instructed concerning the Cu
ban debt and commercial relations, upon
which points they are to insist strenu
ously. All negotiations will be subject to
the direct approval of the Cabinet.
It is said that the instructions are
drawn in the spirit of a compromise.
NEW CHARTER ADOPTED
AT SANTA BARBARA
After Three Attempts in as Many
Years, It Receives a Very
Large Majority.
SANTA BARBARA. Sept. 20.â The new
charter for the city of Santa Barbara was
to-day adopted by a very large majority.
This is the third attempt to adopt a new
charter by this city in as many years.
It is modeled after the charter of New
York city and is modern is every re
spect. The Mayor is given much greater
power. He now will have the power to
appoint and remove the City Marshal and
Assessor, besides many petty officers. The
charter goes into effect in January, 1900.

YOLO POPULIST DBOPS OUT.
C. W. Hatcher Declines to Bun for
the Assembly.
WOODLAND, Sept. 20.â C. W. Hatcher,
middle-of-the-road Populist nominee for
the Assembly, has declined. This leaves
but one nominee, J. R. Mitchell, for the
Assembly, in the field. There ls a big
row in the Populist camp. There were
only three members at the first meeting.
Two of these seceded. The third drummed
up five more a week later, and had him
self nominated for the Assembly. As he
is of Republican antecedents, the only
effect will be to injure the prospects of
Q. W. Pierce, the Republican nominee.

MEN OF THE WINSLOW
REWARDED FOR BRAVERY
Three of the Crew Advanced at the
Recommendation of Their
Commander.
WASHINGTON, Sept. 20.â General or
der 497 issued to-day from the Navy De
partment tells the story of the terrible
fight of the torpedo boat Winslow with
the forts at Cardenas last May, in the
i shape of an official recommendation from
Lieutenant John Bernadou, the command
er of the craft, looking to the advance
ment of Brady, Cooney and Johnson, of
his crew, to warrant officers. This ad
vancement Secretary Long ordered. The
order also quotes from the report of Cap
tain Newcomb of the Hudson his tribute
to the remarkable bravery displayed by
Lieutenant Bernadou and the men of the
, Winslow at the time when many men
'would have felt like abandoning all hope.

JOCKEY THORPE
IN THE SADDLE
Pilots Two Winners at
Sacramento.
FIVE FAVORITES IN FRONT
BING ROUGHLY HANDLED BY
THE BETTORS.
Grady an Easy Victor in the Mile
Mid Fifty Yards Race â Hennessy
Fined by the Judges for
the. Same Old Offense,
Special Dispatch to The Call. '
SACRAMENTO, Sept. 20.-Racing at thte
park this afternoon received fresh im
petus by the reappearance In the saddle
of the premier Western rider, Charley
Thorpe. The "grand old man" rode with
all of his old-time dash and vigor, and
was heartily received by the crowd. Per
haps his effort on Shasta Water was not
a most toothsome turf morsel, but he
piloted Gold Fin and Bonnibel over the
bar safely, and bettors forgave the other.
The ring laid liberal prices against the
different starters, and as live straight
choices hit the wire in front it quit behind
on the day's booking. The sport is above
the average, and the management looks
forward to an increased attendance the
balance of the meeting.
Thorpe donned his riding clothes for
the tirst time since his return here, and
eat astride Gold Fin, the 7 to 10 favorite
In the opening event for two-year-olds.
He was In front all the way, winning han
dily from Correct, a green youngster,
carrying the colors of "Curly" feuields.
For his weak effort on OJai, the second
choice, which finished third, Ruiz was
suspended for one week.
Hermanita was chosen to win the mile
and a sixteenth selling run, and, taking
the lead from Rose Maid and Ringmaster
at the far turn, won in fashionable man
ner. Coda might have made a stronger
bid, but the three pounds over weight
carried by Gus Wilson seriously retarded
her chances. The mare finished an easy
second over Ringmaster.
Quite a cluster" of class and speed faced
the barrier in the one mile and fifty yards
purse race. Grady, with Hennessy up.
was a pronounced favorite, with 9 to 5
chalked against Shasta Water and sixes
about Rey del Tierra, The favorite had
all the speed, and, taking the lead from
Rey del Tlerra three furlongs from home,
won with pounds to spare in l:4o. Thorpe
on Shasta Water allowed himself to be
cut off going down the back stretch, and
was not much of a factor thereafter.
The six-furlong sprint for two-year
olds which followed looked a legacy ror
Bonibel, the 3 to 5 favorite, but it took
every ounce of speed the roan fllly pos
sessed to land. Ocorona, with little Levin
in the saddle, made a bold bid In the
Btretch, and at the wire, after a long
drive, was beaten but a neck. Hannah
Reid, the second choice, hard ridden by
Piggott, finished in the show.
The final Bix-furlong selling affair fell
to William 08, the 1 to 2 choice, piloted
by Frawley. Trappean, ridden by Mike
Hennessy, made all the running and was
nabbed and beaten only in the last few
strides. Hennessy was inclined to claim
too much territory, and was fined $25 for
foul riding Fashion Plate started for the
first time, and received a listless rivie.

with the over-night prices laid here:

SUICIDE OF A WIFE-BEATER.
Jack Wilson Takes a Dose of Mor
phine at Mensem.
VISALJA, Sept. 20.â Jack Wilson, who
has been twice arrested and convicted
of wife-beating, committed suicide by
, taking morphine at Monson Saturday.
Wilson had evidently mado full prepara
tion for committing suicide, as he had
dressed himself in a new suit of clothes
and placed a new pair of shoes beside the
bed. On the porch was found a bottle
containing a small quantity of morphine.
On the 2th of this month he was re
leased from the Fresno County Jail and
it is presumed that remorse for his dis
solute ways so preyed on his mind that
be determined to end his troubles and his
life by committing suicide. He was a
native of England, 46 years of age.
TO FIGHT SCALE BUGS.
Scheme of Southern California Horti
culturists.
LOS ANGELES, Sept. 20.â The South
ern California Horticultural Board has
been organized with representatives
from five counties for the purpose of
adopting means to get rid of the purple
and red scale bugs which are beginning
to Infest the trees. An agent will be
sent to Hawaii and China to secure a
parasite which feeds on these pests. The
Supervisors of the several counties will
be asked to bear the expense of the trip.

THE SAN FRAKCISCO CAI/L, WEDff ESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1893.
PROSPERITY'S ERA
ON THE RANGES
All Kinds of Stock in
Demand.
COMPETITION AMONG BUYERS
BIG EASTERN FIRMS EJNiTER THE
FIELD.
Fair Prices the Rule in the Cattle
Districts of Northern California
and Southeastern
Oregon.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
REDDING, Sept. 20.â Never until the
present season has there been such a
thorough cleaning up of marketable
stock on the great cattle runs of North
ern California, Southeastern Oregon
and portions of Nevada. Only within
the past few years have Eastern buy
ers figured as an important factor In
the stock transactions of these sec
tions. To-day the buying agents of the
great packing houses of Chicago, St.
Paul, Omaha. and Kansas City are ab
solute masters of the situation. They
fix the prices and secure the choice of
the ranges.
Owing to shortages in the Eastern
supply this year the necessities of the
packing interests compelled the buy
ers to scour the most remote stock re
gions in search of beef and mutton,
and territory that has hitherto been
considered as properly belonging to
San Francisco has not been free from
invasion. Prices have consequently
been stiffened, greatly to the advantage
of stockmen. Money is now plentiful
in stock centers and all over the ranges,
not altogether because of enhanced
values, but more on account of the vol
ume of sales effected
For the San Francisco market most
of the cattle secured In extreme North
i era California and Southeastern Ore
gon is shipped from Siskiyou County
points. Edson - rothers are steady ship
pers from Gr-elle. The drought in
Southern California enabled them to
buy in San Luis Obispo 1000 head of
starving cattle. They also bought up
several large bunches of cattle on the
Catalina Islands. This stock was
shipped by rail to Gazelle and turned
out on the well-watered Siskiyou
ranges. These bargains enabled them
to turn off every marketable hoof on
their runs and yet have ample stock
for the succeeding season.
From Ager, Hornbrook and Montague
are shipped all of the stock bought in
Southeastern Oregon for the San Fran
cisco market From one to five special
stock trains a week pass through this
city en route to Sacramento and San
Francisco. Considerable stock is also
shipped from Cottonwood and Red
Bluff.
In Modoc County Eastern buyers ship
cattle and sheep to the packing houses
by way of Reno and the Central route.
Within the past week a bunch of 500
beeves brought $24 50 each all round on
the range. This was in Big Valley.
On Butte Creek sales have been made
lately for $25 a head on the range. A
domestic buyer at Adin secured a bunch
of 450 prime beeves at 4 to 5% cents.
A recent report from Modoc and Las
sen counties says that stockmen have
sold more cattle and sheep this year
than for seven years past, and from
present indications there are enough
buyers in the field to handle every mar
ketable Ijoof in both counties. Not
only are the regular foreign buyers
taking all they can contract for in the
way of beef and mutton, but local men
are buying for speculation.
While the cattle sales are the heav
iest known in years and perhaps in
volve a much larger sum in ready
money, yet the transactions in sheep
are far in excess, head for head, of cat
tle sales. Wool is on the move also.
A sale was made in Lake County, Ore
gon, on the 12th instant of 100,000
pounds at 13% cents.
During the past week 50,000 lambs
have been shipped from Halleck, Ne
vada, to Eastern points. The average
price paid was $2 60 on the range.
From Reno, including drives from. Ore
gon and from Modoc and Lassen coun
ties, 76,000 lambs have been shipped to
the Swift and Armour packing nouses
fn Chicago. The price, in this In
stance, ranged from $2 50 to $2 65, de
livered on the cars.
Montana sheepmen spent twp weeks
on a buying expedition through Grant,
Cook and Gilliam counties, Oregon, and
failed to secure a single head of sheep,
notwithstanding they offered the at
tractive price of $4 80 per pair for ewes
and lambs.
Red Bluff is becoming the head cen
ter for sheep in Northern California.
Sales for the current season have
reached the enormous total of 40,000
head, at prices ranging for mutton
from $2 50 to ?3 25. Lambs brought in
the neighborhood of $2 each. These are
big prices when it is remembered that
up to within a couple of years ago
sheep could hardly be got rid of at half
the price.
At Amadee, a railroad terminus In
Lassen County, the sheep market is
booming. Eastern buyers are taking
everything in sight at $2 50 per head.
From the 10th to the 15th instant P. L.
Flannigan, a local buyer and an ex
tensive flockmaster, transferred to
imoklnÃ h<Â»uiK! agents 40,000 lambs.
The only bad speculation in livestock
that may be noted the present season
was the driving to Nebraska of 600
head of horses from Eastern Oregon.
The best offer received was less than
$2 a head. .

SAN JOSE, Sept. 20.â Frank TV. Fuller

ATTACKED By AN
ENRAGED BUCK
A Thrilling Combat on
Bolinas Ridge.
MAN AND HORSE IN PERU
GORED BY A MONARCH OF THE
GLEN.
Peculiar Circumstances of the En
counter and Lucky Stroke That
Turned the Tide of
Battle.
Special Dispatch to The Call.
SAN RAFAEL. Sept. 20.â Albert Say
ers, proprietor of the San Rafael and
Bolinas stage line, had an encounter
with an enraged deer last evening that
almost resulted in the loss of his life.
The circumstances of the case are
most peculiar.
About 5 o'clock in the afternoon Say
ers was passing down a wooded slope
on Bolinas ridge in a bueerv when an
enraged buck bounded out of the brush
and ran into a wheel of the vehicle with
such force that* it. was thrown to the
ground. Maddened with the pain of the
collision, it rose to its feet and ran at
the horse, trying to thrust its horns into
the animal's side. Fearing that his
horse, a valuable animal, would be dis
emboweled, Sayers drew a pocket-knife
and leaped out of the buggy.
At the first jab of the knife the in
furiated buck turned on Sayers, who
was knocked down by the fierce on
slaught. He managed to secure a hold
on one of the antlers an! tried to cut
the deer's throat, but could not get in
an effectual blow. The struggle was so
furious that Sayers was soon winded
and nearly at the mercy of the angry
buck, when a lucky opportunity pre
sented itself and he drove the knife
deep into its neck. At this juncture the
deer gave the whistle peculiar to its
species, and, with a parting stroke of
its hoof that gashed Sayers' hand,
broke away and fled into the forest.
During the encounter the horse stood
perfectly still, evidently palsied with
fright. Its sides were badly torn by the
buck's horns, and Sayers himself suf
fered numerous cuts and bruises and
had his trousers shredded by the deer's
hoofs.
DISASTROUS TYPHOONS
ON THE JAPANESE COAST
Collisions at Sea, Shipwrecks and
Loss of Life â Urging Claims
Against Hawaii.
VICTORIA, B. C, Sept. 20.â H. M. S.
Empress of India arrived to-day bringing
news that serious typhoons have done
great -damage on the Japanese coast. In
Yokohama harbor the* British ship Lind
hurst and the Italian cruiser Marco Polo
collided, both being damaged.
The wreck of the American ship Emily
"W. Whitney is reported from Shanghai,
and a number of the crew were lost. She
was lost in a typhoon, which caused the
abandonment of the German ship Trini
dad.
Roporu from Canton say tne revolu
tion is assuming alarming proportions.
Japanese papers urge that more dam
ages be demanded from Hawaii.
REDLANDS WATER FAMINE.
REDLAN.DS, Sept. 20.â Redlands Is
threatened with a water famine. The
users of irrigation water have been noti
fied by the Bear Valley Water Com
pany that they will be given but one
fourteenth the usual supply for use on
orchards the rest of this season. The
supply In Bear Valley is very low. Urir
less rains set in soon not only the pres
ent crop of oranges will be lost,. but also
the trees will be killed.
The domestic water company took off
its sprinkling carts to-day and stopped all
use of water for power purposes. Con
sumers will be given but two hours a
week to irrigate their lawns and trees.
A mass meeting of citizens last night
denounced the Bear Valley sale of
water to outsiders as robbery and ap
pointed a committee to devise ways and
means to secure an adequate water sup
ply for irrigation and domestic use.
Residents are being supplied with drink
ing water pumped from deep wells and
peddled from house to house In carts.
