men themselves suspect for their feel
ing of unrest and discouragement.
They are not "hard up," but "busted"â
that's the plain truth. We are five
miles from town, and the carfare there
and return is 20 cents, so that the men
are thrown on their own resources and
are compelled to remain in camp. They
have been used to the freedom of San
Francisco, with its myriad attractions,
and the contrast is disagreeable. Most
of the men, I know, are practically pen
niless, and to be so in what is practi
cally a foreign country is, to say the
least, tantalizing. But conditions are
bound to improve; they are im
proving. August pay is expected
in a week's time. Its arrival
will make all the difference in the
world. Company E has started a com
pany canteen, supported by the com
pany fund. Each man is given a ticket,
to be paid for on the arrival of his pay,
good for $1 in trade at the canteen,
where tobacco, pies, soft drinks, con-
densed milk, etc., are sold at less than
usual retail rates. It is the general
verdict in the company street that it is
the best institution they have yet or
ganized. All have the privilege of
bathing at Waikiki, with its fine sandy
beach and delightfully warm water.
And the necessity in this warm climate
of bathing is turned into our chief
pastime, many going in twice a day
and remaining several hours at a time.
Brown canvas suits have been issued,
so that, although neatness In a blue
uniform would be out of the question,
the brown can be used for loafing about
in camp and for drilling and the blue
saved for dress. Canvas uniforms, too,
are very cool and durable, which is an
important consideration in making the
many excursions through thickets and
jungles and over rocks and mountains
which the men are accustomed, to make
while off duty. Sunday is practically
a holiday, as there is no duty from
reveille to retreat calls. And during
that interval few are found in camp.
Large watering carts are now driven
through the camp at frequent intervals
through the day, tending thereby very
materially to solve the dust question.
On September 4 an important order
was issued by command of Major Gen
eral Merriam, constituting a military
district of all the Hawaiian territory,
with headquarters at Honolulu and
with General Charles King in com
mand. All troops present, numbering
nearly 3000 officers and men, were con
solidated for administration into two
camps â one, Camp McKinley, un
der command of Colonel Thomas H.
Barber, comprising the First Infantry,
New York Volunteers, and the battalion
of Second United States Volunteer En
gineers, commanded by Major Lang
fitt; the other, called Camp otis, being
made up of all expeditionary forces
here, the commanding officer being the
senior officer of those forces present.
This leaves Colonel Barber, the First
New York and the Volunteer Engineers
still the permanent garrison of the mili
tary post which they came out to
establish, and which it seems as
though they were to maintain. General
Merriam returns to-day to San Fran
cisco by the Australia, and undoubt
edly his experience and observations
here will be the foundation for sugges
tions and reports to Washington which
will materialize here in "orders" in two
weeks' time, more or less.
JUDGE ANTONE ROSA
SUCCUMBS TO DROPSY
HONOLULU, Sept. 13.â Antone Rosa,
the well-known Hawaiian lawyer, ex-
Attorney General and ex-Circuit
Judge, died on the 9th, of dropsy, be
ing within a few weeks of his forty
third year. He has been seriously ill
for some time past and his death was
not unexpected. He leaves a widow and
three small children, and a brother,
George Rosa, now in the Auditor Gen
eral's office.
Princess Kaiulani's reception In
honor of the American-Hawaiian Com
mission party given at Ainahau on thp
7th, was a brilliant success. General
Merriam and staff, Admiral Miller and
staff, General King and staff, Colonel
Barber and staff, Government offi
cials, army and navy officers, ex-Queen
Liliuokalani and several hundred
prominent society people of the city
attended.
The United States troopship Arizona
sailed for Manila at 5 o'clock last even
ing. A lot of mail for the soldiers in the
Philippines and the fleet was taken
aboard Just previous to the sailing.
Stores and coal had been loaded on
Saturday, and everything was ready
for sailing yesterday morning save a
few little odds and ends. The follow
ing were the passengers on the Arizona
for Manila: Dr. Corbusler, Lieutenant
Boutelle, Lieutenant Colonel Barnett,
Lieutenant James, Lieutenant Colonel
Martin, Captain Evans, Lieutenant
Lanza, Lieutenant Wright, Mrs. Rus
sell. Mrs. C. McC. Reeve, Miss Russell,
Miss Laswell, Miss Garlick, Mrs. Dr.
Woods, Mrs. Dr. Beere, Miss Killian,
Mrs. Palm, Miss Rolph, Mrs. Leonard,
Miss Mythe, Mrs. Woodruff and son,
Miss Albertin Schaefer. Mr. Dwight,
Mr. Cadwell, Mr. Leonard. Mr. Kubel,
Mr. Durst and Mr. Tanner.
Passing of a Humboldt Pioneer.
EUREKA, Sept. 20.â Captain Edwin
Tomlinson, a pioneer, aged 89, died at an
early hour this morning. Tomlinson was
a retired sea captain, English by birth.
He was a highly respected citizen of this
community and a member of the Hum
boldt Pioneers.

COMMERCIAL WORLD.
SUMMARY OF THE ; MARKET.
Sliver firmer.
Wheat, Barley and Oats advanced again.
Corn and Rye unchanged.
Hay rules linn.
Bayo Beans declined again.
Potatoes and Onions about the same.
Eggs advanced again. .
Butter and Cheese- steady.
Two cars of Eastern Poultry In. â â  .
Fresh Fruits steady and Grapes better.
Dried Fruits show no further Change.
Prices for new Almonds established.
Provisions quiet.
Hops firm. Hides and Wool dull.
Hogs show more firmness.
Merchandise quotations unaltered.
EXPORTS OF WHEAT AND FLOUR.
Exports of Flour from this port during the
first eight months of the year were 545,842 bbls.
against 592,670 during the same "time in 1897.
Reducing this Flour to a Wheat basis and add
ing it to the exported Wheat gives total Wheat
exports for the time named of 5,049,225 ctls,
valued at $7,435,500, ' against 5,483,700 ctls at
136, 000 in 1897.

WEATHER CONDITIONS AND GENERAL
FORECAST.
A storm of marked energy for this season of
the year appears off the coast of Washington
this evening. The pressure has fallen over
Washington and Oregon quite rapidly during
the past twelve hours.
The temperature has fallen over Nevada,
Utah and Idaho. There has been a slight rise
In Central Washington and Oregon. The tem
peratures in California are slightly below the
normal. . ......
The weather is cloudy and partly cloudy over
Washington, Oregon, Idaho, Nevada, Utah and
along the California coast north of San Fran-
Cisco. In the southern part of the Pacific Slope
the weather Is clear. . â  â -â¢â â  Â.-.v.
Rain is reported from all stations in Western
Washington.
A maximum wind velocity of 44 miles per
hour from the southeast is reported from Fort
Can by.
Rain is not expected south of Cape Mendoclno
on the coast and the Oregon line in the interior.
Forecast made at San Francisco for thirty
hours ending midnight, September 21, 1898:
Northern Californiaâ Partly cloudy Wednes
day, probably with light showers along the ex
treme northwestern coast; brisk southwest
wind.
Southern Californiaâ Fair Wednesday; fresh
west wind.
Nevadaâ Cloudy and probably light showers
In the northern portion; fair In the southern
portion Wednesday.
Utahâ Cloudy, with showers Wednesday.
Arizona â Wednesday.
San Francisco and Vicinityâ Cloudy Wednes
day; brisk southwest wind.
Special from Mount Tamalpals â Clear; wind
west.' 24 miles; temperature. 56: maximum tem
perature, 64. G. H. WILLSON,
Local Forecast Official..
EASTERN MARKETS.
NEW YORK STOCK MARKET.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â The stock market to
day presented all the characteristics of genuine
reaction. Last week's sharp recovery elimi
nated the short Interests very largely and the
forced liquidation for profits had been mostly
completed during the previous decline. The
market was thus freed to a great extent from
technical Influences and in condition to re
spond to the prospects for general business in
the country. Its failure to do so yesterday was
attributed to the unsettling Influences of the
slump In Sugar. The bears were timid about
continuing their campaign further against
Â£ugar and were Inclined to cover, so that thia
Influence toward reaction was removed. Never
theless, the market drooped heavily toward a
lower basis with one or two exceptions In the
list and closed only barely steady at a level
generally of 1 per cent or more below last
night's close. The action of the market waa
not different in character from that which
prevails at a time when the business outlook
presents general discouragements, contraction
of activity on declining profits. The demand
for stocks was very small all day and prices
went down easily under comparatively light
offerings. Weakness was particularly mani
fest In Tobacco, the grangers and Pacifies.
The action of these stocks indicated the liqui
dation of a heavy Individual speculation on
the long side. The sharpest break In the
market was coincident of the marking up of
call loans to 6 per cent. This flurry in the
money market was a disappointment to the bull
operators, and especially in view of the en
gagement of over $2,0<>0,0G0 for import and the
mailing from the Treasury Department of
checks for interest on registered 4 per cent
bonds due October 1, amounting to J4, 910,000,
which are payable immediately. The sub
treasury's debit balance at the clearing-house
also ran up to-day to $1,261,458, reflecting a
large increase in Government expenditures.
The sluggishness of the gold movement is
somewhat remarkable in face of conditions.
Call money In London Is % per cent against 6
per cent here to-day. London discount is 1%,
to 1"* against 4 per cent here. Last month's
trade balance in this country's favor was
$35,w0,000 on top of the favorable balance for
every month in the preceding year. In ad
dition London has recently bought securities
in this market. It is a fact that part of to
day's gold engagements were made by bullion
dealers here who are shipping silver in re
sponse to the active demand attributed to
Spanish account. There is not sufficient de
mand for the exchange against the silver ship
ment to make a market and there is a better
profit in importing gold. Exchange is thus
clearly demonstrated to be at the gold import
point and the question arises what has been
done with the country's credits abroad that
they do not bring a larger gold movement.
The immediate future of the local money mar
ket Is, however, clearly toward greater ease.
Dealings in bonds were not on a large scale
to-day arid prices were toward a lower level.
Total sales, 51.510.170.
United States 4s. old and new, were % lower
In the bid price to-day.

shares, including: 4085 At<-hlnon preferred; 14.

LONDON MARKET.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20â The Evening Post's
London financial cablegram says: The stock
markets opened steady here to-day, but cloeed
generally dull on the unexpectedly large efflux
of gold from the Bank of. England. Americans
were good at one time on German orders to
buy Erie, Union Pacific and Northern Pacific
common. The other markets hardened sympa
thetically, but there was no public support
here and the close was dull in the absence of
New York buying. A small feature is the re
vival of speculation in nitrate shares.
Spanish fours were firm on Paris buying and
Spanish exchange In London fell sharply to
39 80, against 40 v SB at the opening of the
month. Upon information from an excellent
quarter the negotiations with the Hongkong
Bank and Chinese Government for railway
loam arÂ»> proceeding most satisfactorily for
British Interests.
Consols were lower on the gold movement
and on the confirmed report that the Bank of
England is seeking to get control of the money
market by borrowing on consols. This point,
if true, will be most important, but cannot ex
press a definite opinion at the time of cabling- I
The total efflux of gold from the Bank to
day makes f742.000. which, the official state- |
ment shows, Included Â£397.000 In bar gold and i
Â£215.000 in United States coin. All is said to
be going to New York, l>ut I have reason to
believe that a portion of the bar gold has gone
to Germany. \

â  " â  . â - .x â 
CHICAGO, Sept. 20.â A surprise was given
traders in wheat at the outset by the big drop

apolis and Duluth reporting but 820 cars, com
pared with 1993 yesterday and 1924 a year ago.

a slight reaction. A period of dullness ensued,
with prices varying but little until about noon,
when the Atlantic . port clearance figures were
posted, showing the surprisingly large , total ; of
977,000 bushels, of which New York contributed
606,000 bushels. As yesterday's visible* supply
statement gave stocks at New York only 188,000
bushels, to-day's big shipments were entirely
unlocked for and a buying flurry ensued imme
diately, I December advancing to 63c and Sep
tember to 66% c, with the demand . quite gen
eral. Bradetreet's figures on the world's visi
ble, showing an increase of "3,692,000 bushels,
encouraged sellers again and caused prices to
drop a little, December getting back to 62% c,
but near the close the strength shown . by out
side - markets, especially for the cash article,
started buying again and the market from that
time on ruled very firm. December was finally
bid to 63H@63%c and closed at 63% c, l%c higher
than - yesterday. September > advanced to 66% c
and closed at 66% c, l%c: higher than yesterday.

closed %c higher than yesterday. -

AVAILABLE GRAIN SUPPLY.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â Special cable and
telegraphic dispatches to Bradstreets indicate
the following changes in available supplies last
Saturday as compared with the preceding
Sataiday.
Wheat, United States and Canada, east of the
Rockies, Increase 3,092,000; Liverpool Corn
Trade News afloat for and in Europe, Increase
600,000. Total supply, total Increase, 3.692,000.
Corn, United States and Canada, East Rock
ies increased 2,42t5,000.
Oats, United States and Canada, East Rock
ies increased 104, 000.
The expected rush of spring wheat to market
materialized in the Canadian northwest this
week, and it Is significant of the development
of new points of accumulation of recent years,
that BradÂ«treets' report Increases in points ex
clusively reported by It, aggregating almost aÂ»
large an amount as that reported at older
points of accumulation by the regular official
report.
Among the more Important gains as given
by Bradstreets, not reported in the official vis
ible supply statement, are the increases of 900,
000 bushels at Manitoba storage points; 140,000
bushels at New Orleans; 122,000 bushels at Fort
William and Keewating; 55, 000 bushels at Chi
cago private elevators and 50,000 bushels at
northwestern interior elevators. The aggregate
stock of wheat held at Portland, Or., and Se
attle and Tacoma, show an increase of 103,000
birthels over last week.

CALIFORNIA RAISINS.
NEW YORK, Sept. 20.â The Journal of Com
merce says: The present situation in Califor
nia raisins does not seem to be at all satisfac
tory to commission merchants in the East.
Accepting prices and terms made by the Cali
fornia Raisin-Growers' Association they have
gone ahead and sold freely for September ship
ment, and In a very short time we understand
all that Is available for September shipment
had been contracted for. Then sellers in the
Eastern market solicited and obtained orders
for the first half of the October shipment, but
before many of these orders were confirmed
notice was sent out by the association that the
time for shipments at the opening prices had
been extended to midnight, October 89 only, af
ter which it was probable that an advance of
He per pound would be made by the associa
tion, and all goods shiped after that date would
be subject to Increased price. Sellers are in
doubt as to whether the increased prices will
apply to goods that were ordered before October
8, arid which could not be shipped on or before
that date or only to orders for the October
shipment that might be received on the Bth of
thnt month or thereafter. If first, merchants
here claim unjust advantage will be taken by
the association in putting higher price on or
ders that wpre received, could not be con
firmed, before the time at which the new prices
would go Into effect. A good deal of telegraph
ing to the coast was done to-day, we under
stand, with a view to ascertaining Just what
sellers here are to expect, but.up to the close
nothing definite seemed to have been obtained.
Many of the local commission merchants seem
to think thnt they have not been well treated
by the association, and some of them go so
far as to intimate that If the present course
of that organization Is persisted In, Its Influ
ence may soon be lost.

HAY AND FEEDSTUFFS.
Receipts of Hay fell oft sharply to 240 tons,
and there would probably have been a further
advance had it not looked like rain, which de
terred eelllers from exacting too large prices.
It will be a weather market from now on. If
It rains the market will probably ease off, ana
if it does not, still better prices will doubtless
result.
Feedstuffs remain the same.

THE STOCK MARKET.
There was less change In mining stocks,
though the few variations were generally In
the direction of weaker prices.
There was no particular change In local se
curities, though the Sugar stocks were weak.
The Homestake Mining Company of South
Dakota will pay a monthly dividend of 60 cents
per share on September 26.
The following ore shipments were made
from the mines of Eureka and Hamilton dis
tricts, Nevada, by rail for the week ending
September 16: Eureka districtâ Diamond mine,
64,280 pounds; Excelsior, 32,050; Hidden Jewel
and Laurel, 46,250; Jackson, 98,200; Richmond.
34,800; M. Fnrllnger, 3520. From Hamiltonâ
McEllln, 44,640; Rocco (Lani & Co.), 141,310;
Rocco (Smith & Jackson), 71,410.
The official letter from the Occidental Con
solidated mine for the past week says: "From
the etopes above the 600 level extracted and
milled 120 tons of ore, and from the 450 level we
have extracted and sent to the mill 17 tons.
The average assay value of all the ore worked
was $11 20 per ton."
Special meetings of stockholders to reduce
the capitalization will be held by the Consoli
dated California and Virginia. Union Consoli
dated and Alpha Consolidated mining compa
nies to-day. The par value of the stock of the
Consolidated California and Virginia and
Union Consolidated will be reduced to $2 50 per
share, and of the Alpha Consolidated to $1 per
share, from the present rate of $100 per share.
The Comstock Pumping Association has or
gnnized by electing Charles Hirschfeld presi
dent and chairman, August Waterman secre
tary and Gilbert McM. Roes superintendent, to
represent the association on the Comstock
lode. The following advisory committee, rep
resenting every controlling Interest on the Com-
Btock, will act with Chairman Hlrschfeld in
carrying out the objects of the association:
Charles H. Fish, George R. Wells, W. G. Mor
row, John Landers, Thomas Cole, James New
lands and C. 1* McCoy. It is estimated that
$100,000 will be needed to carry out the first
work.

NOTEâ In the above exposition of the tide*
the early morning tides are given in the left
hand column and the successive tides of the
day In the order of occurrence as to time. The
second time column gives the second tide of
the day, the third time column the third tide
and the last or Hght hand column gives the.
last tide of the day, except when there are but
three tides as sometimes occur. The heights
given are additions to the soundings on the
United States Coast Survey charts, except
when a minus sign (â ) precedes the height,
and then the number given Is subtracted from
the depth given by the charts. The plane of
reference Is the mean of the lower low waters.

TOO HASTY
Severely Arraigns the

NO POLITICS IN JUDAISM
WAy MARCTJSE REPLIES TO DR.
FRIEDLANDER.
Joseph Harris Says That the Sixth
Ward Club Is Working Solely
for Good Govern
ment.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 20.
Several prominent members of the Jew-.
Ish faith are incensed at a statement
made to-day by Dr. Friedlander regard-
Ing the Forty-eighth Assembly district
Good Government club. 'xhis club was
formed a few weeks ago for the purpose
of promoting good government and re
moving certain complaints that had been
made regarding that portion of the local
Hebrews who reside in the Sixth Ward.
Rabbi Friedlander said:
A poHticai club consisting of Jews for Jewish
interests If not the sense ut the Jewish commu
nity of OaJUand. nut the sense of the Jewish
fraternity at larsj.-. It Is not sanctioned by
Jewism. Fvery Jew in this country enjoys
equal rights In Identifying and amalgamating
hIF political influence and interests wfth the
rest of the American citizens, Irrespective
of creed, and while the intention of the people
composing the club Is not questioned, the i in
side world may misconstrue it and bring upon
the Jew the undeserved criticism of clannish-
ness. . .'
The First Hebrew Congregation of Oakland,
which is substantially the Jewish community
of the city, discountenances any political or
ganization, composed distinctively of Jews, for
Jewish political interests.-
V li ire the Jewish sentiment, I appeal to the
Forty-eighth Assembly District Club-to disband
mid continue manifesting the same 'confidence
In the sense of fairness of those leaders and
citizens of the country. State and county, who
thus far have been recognizing and distributing
political distinctions to those who merit -them.
Joseph Harris, who was a candidate for
the Assembly, and who is a member of
the club, says Dr. Friedlander is entirely
at sea regarding the purpose ior which
the club was formod. "In the Sixth
Ward." said Mr. Harris, "there ts a large
number of the poorer classes of Jews. It

FOUND A FRIEND IN NEED.
The Martha W, Tuft From Petedland With Mahogany Logs Ran Short of Provisions on the Way
Here. The Bark B, P. Cheney From the Canneries Met Her Off Point Reyes and
Gave Her a 'Supply of Canned Salmon.
Tuft had a very hrmi time at It on the
way here from Petedland Ray, a small
place, about 110 mil* io of Aca
pulco. The provlsioi â¢ â  gave oirf and
for days sill th^re was to eat was a small
portion at bread madi from Hour and
water. Luckily tt â¢ :. u_ p. Cheney,
from the canneries, v... , ; . â¢ H jth outside
and a supply of canned salmon was pro
cured.
The Tuft left hore over four months ago
for ports in Mexico. She was nupposed
to have had at least four months' provi
elons aboard, but the men Â«ay she did
not have sufficient food for three. Before
leaving Petedland Bay the rook says he
told Captain Johnson that there was not
enough provisions aboard to last to ban
Francisco.
The captain could get nothing in the
shape of food at that out-of-the-way
place, so when his cargo of mahogany
logs was aboard he had to start with the
larder depleted. The canned meats were
the first to give out and then the beans i
followed suit. The men, declare they were |
has been common talk for years that they
could always be herded at election time
and their votes obtained at prices vary
ing from 50 cents to a dollar. These re
ports have become irksome, and in order
to rÂ« move them and prove that the Jews
In that ward are as independent politi
cally as any other class of citizens, they
formed a good government club. The
organization is not. as the Rabbi pre-
Bumes, for the purpose of furthering Jew
ish Interests politically, but for a much
morn worthy cause, and I do not think
the members will disband merely because
Dr - Friedlander suggests it."
Max Marcuse, a prominent Hebrew,
says, that Dr. Friedfander had better
have kept quiet. "I do not see why he
should have rushed into print," said Max
Marcuse, "before he knew what he was
talking about. On several occasions Dr.
rriedlander has opened his mouth and
put his foot Into it, and this time is one
of the occasions. In the Sixth Ward,
where the club was formed, there is quite
a number of Russian Jews, and they are
anxious to disprove certain rumors cir
culated regarding them, and Dr. Fried
lander, who Is not the rabbi or that con
gTegation, would probably have done bet
ter had he encouraged them Instead of
speaking as he has done."
Mrs. Simons Sues for Divorce.
OAKLAND, Sept. 20.â Nancy B. Simons,
daughter of M. C. Culver, who a year ago
married Ernest G. Simons, a popular
young Alamedan, is now suing for divorce
on the ground of desertion. Last July a
divorce complaint was filed by Attorney
R. B. Tappan on behalf of Mrs. Simons,
but when it developed that the attorney
was representing both sides Hugh Aldrich
Was substituted as counsel for the wife,
and on August 15 the case was dismissed.
To-day, however, a bona-fide suit was
filed by Attorney Aidrich, while it is ex
pected that Tappan will represent the
defense. Mrs. Simons sues for $250 month
ly alimony and $250 counsel fees, besides
the divorce. The case promises to be an
interesting one.
She Wants to Be a Citizen.
OAKLAND, Sept. 20.â Miss Ethel L.
Cumbers, a native of England and resid
ing in San Francisco, appeared before
Judge Ogden to-day and declared her in
tention of becoming a citizen. The young
lady, in her endeavors to secure a posi
tion in the civil service, ran up against
the statutory requirement which provides
that all holders of Federal positions must
be citizens of the United States. She has
a promise of a position, and hence the ap
plication.

Doctor Not to Blame.
OAKLAND, Sept. 20.â At the Inquest
over the remains of Fritz Jausch in
Berkeley this morning the jury returned
a verdict of death due to congestion of the
lungs following diphtheria, and also found
that deceased had beon properly treated
by Dr. D. de Marconnay.

THE SAN FEANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1898.
THE WIDOW
NOW REFUSES
TO MARRY
Mrs. Flaherty Is- Sued
by Her Lover.
WANTS THE PRESENTS BACK
PREFERS A SURE PENSION TO A
HUSBAND.
Lover Gregg Believes He Should Be
Given the Value of His
Services and
Loans.
Oakland Office San Francisco Call,
908 Broadway, Sept. 20.
Mrs. Mary Flaherty, who has frequently
been a party to suits In the Police Court,
has been toying, so he says, with the
heart of John Gregg, a laborer of Temes
cal. Gregg and the widow have been mak
ing love to each other for several months,
and now Gregg Is out over $250 on the
agreement, and has Invoked the aid of the
Justice's Court to bring the lady to time.
John Gregg llled a complaint this after
noon, In which he alleges that Mrs. Fla
herty agreed to marry him, but that after
he had advanced her over $250 she broke
the agreement. Now, because he cannot
have the widow, he wants the money.
The complaint alleges that in May, two
years ago, Gregg and Widow Flaherty
agreed to marry, but were cautious
enough to reserve the right to rescind
their contract upon notice to the other of
such intention. In such event, it was
agreed, so the complaint says, that the
Widow Flaherty should return and re
store to plaintiff all moneys and proper
ties given to her by Gregg. To be very
exact, Gregg says that the money, goods
and labor with which he has furnished
her are worth $268 55.
It appears that about the time of the
agreement the comely widow was nego
tiating for a pension, and after learning
that in the event of her marriage her pen
sion would cease she served Gregg with a
verbal notice that the agreement to marry
must be forgotten. Gregg did not accept
this situation, believing that his services
as a husband were worth more than the
$12 a month which his fiancee would re
BRIDE OF J\
CO.NSTROCTOR
Herbert Francia Phelps and Miss Jean
Evelyn McKee were married at noon
on Saturday last by the Rev. James
Wood worth. Mr. Phelps Is In charge of
the department of construction of the
sugar manufactory now In process of
completion for the Spreckels Sugar Com
pany at the town of Spreckels, In Mon
terey County. The bride is the daugh
ter of the late John McKee of the Tallant
Company Bank.
The wedding took place at the residence
of Mrs. McKee, on Valencia street, near
Eighteenth, where the bride has for soma
time made her home.
Owing to recent deaths in the family
the wedding was a quiet one. only rela
tives and Immediate friends being pres
ent.
The young couple received many pres
ents, prominent among which was a sil
ver tea service, the gift of the groom's
business associates. Mr. and Mra. Phelps
will make their home at Spreckels.
On Wednesday evening last Miss Carry
Goldman and Benedict Fleischer were>
marriod, the Rev. Dr. Vooraanger offi
ciating.
The ceremony took place In the ban
quet hall at Delmonico's, after which
the guests, one hundred In number, par
took of an eftihorate dinner.
The bridal procession was led by two
little flower girlsâ Miss Mn.e Neuman and
Miss Sadie Sternâ who strewed the bride's
path with roses. Sanford Walter was
best man, and the maid of honor was
Miss Hilda Kline. The bride wore a gown
of white organdie over white taffeta. The
maid of honor was gowned in pink and
white organdie. The newly wedded pair
have pone on an extended tour through
Southern California.

The Body of an Unknown.
OAKLAND, Sept. 20.â The remains of
an unknown man were found In San Le
andro Bay, near the town of that name
this morning. The body was badly de
composed and there was nothing about
the clothing by which to establish iden
tity and the coat and vest were missing.
Coroner Baldwin will hold an inquest to
morrow afternoon.
Brehm Is Sleeted.
BERKELEY, Sept. 20.â At a meeting of
the class of 1900 of the University of Cali
fornia this afternoon George Brehm was
elected manager "of the Blue and Gold in
place of I. J. Muraa, who resigned re
cently. J. B. Dunlap, editor of the an
nual, asked for a guarantee fund beforo

Berkeley News Notes.
BERKELEY, Sept. 20.â The Board of
Education has approved the action of Su
perintendent Perham in ordering the Co
lumbia School to be closed for one week
longer. The move was taken owing to the
prevalence of diphtheria in the district,
as several of the parents refuse to allow
their children to attend.
The election of officers for the Athletic
Association of the University of Califor
nia will be held to-morrow between the
hours of 9 a. m. and 3:45 p. m. The con
test promies to be one of the most ex
citing that has occurred In the college
for some time past. Votes will be cast
by both the men and women students.
The Rev. H. H. Dobbins has been
chosen as pastor of the South Berkeley
Presbyterian Church. The congregation
has purchased a lot and expect to erect
a house of worship as soon as Bufflclent
funds can be obtained.
CITY TRUSTEES.
Street Improvement Contracts Let.
Objections to a New Laundry
on Railroad Avenue.
ALAMEDA, Sept. 20.â At the meeting
of the City Trustees last evening Daston
& Wilson were awarded the contract for
constructing a sewer in SevMth street
and the same firm was awardm the con
tract for grading, paving and sidewalking
Encinal street.
A strong protest against permitting the
establishment of another laundry within
the limits of Railroad and Encinal ave

mantels. Mrs. Ixsvell White, the dlgni

FORGERY IS ALLEGED.
Bupert Manuel, a Law Clerk, Accused
of Probating a Will Purporting
to Be That of His Wife.
Garret Van Ness, 1137 Florida street,
swore to a complaint In Judge Low's
court yesterday for the arrest of Rupert
Manuel on the charge of forgery.
Manuel is a law clerk and hl.s wife died
In January last. On April 5 Manuel filed
her will for prohate. It read as follows:
"In the name of the good God, I, Annie
Manuel, wife of Rupert Manuel, of sound
mind, do make this my will. I give all
that I have In this world to my dear hus
band, Rupert Manuel, as all that I have
was made since our marriage, and is com
munity property. I leave my little niece,
Frances Van Ness, under his care, as 1
am sure he will take care of her."
The will was dated January 2. and the
complnlnlnp witness alleges that It was
forged by Manuel after his wife was dead.
He declares that ho and his brother,
James Van Ness, are the real heirs at
law of Mrs. Manuel, who was their sister,
and they are entitled to the property left
by her " which amounts to between 12500
and fciWO. Attorney Collier represents the
complaining witness^

THE SAN FRANCISCO CALL, WEDNESDAY, SEPTEMBER 21, 1898.
SAYS THOUGHT
IS KING OF
THE WORLD
It Rules Man and All
His Affairs.
A HEALER OF ALL ILLS
DOCTRINE OF CHRISTIAN SCI
ENCE EXPLAINED.
Lecture by Edward A. Kimball of
Chicago on the Power
of Faith and
Mind.
Edward A. Klmball of Chicago lec
tured before a large audience in Golden
Gate Hall last evening on the subject
of "Christian Science."
After referring to the fact that he
himself and tens of thousands of others
had been healed by this science, Mr.
Kimball said:
One of the most important features of
Christian science is the exposition of
mental causation as the procurator of
disease.
In order to properly direct your atten
tion I wish to remind you of the fact that
thought governs humanity at all points.
All the strife and war among nations
is, primarily, thought. All the business
affairs of the world are thus originated.
Continents are discovered, explored and
colonized; nations are founded; cities are
built; the earth is made to bring forth its
products; goods are designed and manu
factured and all of the vast, ponderous
transactions of the world are procured
and continued by the action of thought.
Thought establishes social relationships,
and, on the other hand, it murders and
commit* suicide. It brings forth the
manifestations of music, art and inven
tion, and. again, it demolishes and de
stroys, without thought all human activ
ity would, cease; the earth would become
depopulated; the wheels of industry would
abruptly stop, crumble and decay and the
work or man's hands be obliterated, and
mortal man and his affairs, which exist
because of thought, would be a nonentity
without it.
When you understand that thought
works out such a tremendous impulsion
in the career of man you will find little
difficulty in accepting the proposition in
Christian Science, that thought which
does, everything else to a human being
can also make him sick.
Such an admission paves the way to
the acceptance of a correlative statement
that if evil and ignorant thought, fear,
hatred and passion, can make him sick,
true thought, that t>hich is natural and
scientific, can bring about a changed con
dition and produce a healing influence
and effect.
Christian Science points out the possi
bility of your obtaining complete mastery
over the sense of pain and disease, and j
Just in proportion as you gain this under- i
standing and thereby manifest the nat
ural dominion and control over bodily
conditions you will gradually lose all ;
conscious and unconscious fear and es- I
cape its havoc.
The change of thought which must and
will transform the world will reverse all
of the purely material theories about dis- j
ease and show that many of the steps j
that are taken to prevent and cure it but j
aggravate and augment it. The new un- i
derstanding of scientific law will annul !
the laws of disease, will locate mental
causation and remove the consequences
of fear.
Evil thought concerning disease has an
offspring in evil conversation about it,
and to-day the race is inflicting the most
appalling consequences upon Itself as the
result of insufferable and limitless talk
about sickness.
People who fear and avoid contagion
and infection will actually submerge
themselves in the introspection of self
and the inspection and comments of every I
one else, not knowing that those things !
are more harmful and do vastly more
damage than the others.
Medical records indicate Innumerable
instances where illness has terminated j
fatally simply because of the fright and i
discouragement that were projected upon
the patient by other people who through
narrative, prediction or expressed fears
prostrated the hope and courage and ex
pectation of the sick man who, if undis
turbed, would have recovered spontane
ously.
Christian Scientists urge the avoidance
of the world-wide habit of overmuch talk
ing about the diseased condition of the
body, because as a theme for conversa
tion It is or should be unattractive and
offensive to people of wholesome sensi
bilities. Secondly, because nearly all such
conversation is among people who know
really nothing about disease; and lastly,
because such conversations propagate
fear and plant the seeds of disorder and
disease, which unconsciously lodge, ger
minate and consume.
Christian Science has reformed drunk
ards and the victims of morphine and
other habits. Confirmed or chronic in
ebriety is among the Infirmities of man
which have been considered well nigh
hopeless.
Christian Science heals all sorts of peo
ple of all manner of diseases. At the
World's Congress of Religions it was au
thoritatively stated that more than one
million cases of disease had been healed
by it up to that time and that many of
them had been considered incurable. It
has cured diseases that have never been
known to be healed by any other means.
It has instructed the infidel and agnostic,
who, unable to adopt any of the miscon
ceived substitutes for Deity which have
disfigured human thought, have been glad
to acquaint themselves with the under
standing of God which unfolds the very
substance and science of being, instead of
involving the believer in a labyrinth of
mystery which gives no promise of yield
ing to anything but death.
Christian Science has purified and ex
alted the motives, desires and alms of
men. Increased their wisdom, their exec
utive and business capacity; arrested tne
havoc of vice; refined character and ele
vated manhood and citizenship. These are j
indisputable facts, and they are indelibly I
engraved on the annals of humanity. To
dispute them now would be folly. I leave i
them with you without even entreating |
you to give heed, buÂ» 'leing persuaded
that the wise man will in due time ms
cern that a happipr destiny awaits adop
tion by mortal man and with the confi
dence that toward that destiny the weary
sufferer will at last turn his footsteps
to realize the deliverance which has fcoen
the hope of the ages.

ROSSER HELD
ON THE CHARGE
OF MURDER
Preliminary Hearing
in the Police Court.
EVIDENCE OF THE SHOOTING
NO DEFENSE OFFERED FOB THE
TENNESSEEAN.
During the Examination He Sat in
His Chair Dejected and Ap
parently Without
Hope.
Walter Rosser, the young Tennessee
soldier, charged with the murder of
Henry Hilderbrand in the Spreckels Mar
ket on September 13, was the picture <t
despair as he sat in Judge Joachlmscn's
court yesterday morning listening to the
testimony of the witnesses that may send
him to the gallows.
The court room was crowded with the
usual morbid throng of people, but if
they expected any startling development
they were disappointed. The preliminary
examination was pushed through ex
pedltlously and no time was wasted in
asking Irrelevant questions. Prosecut
i ing Attorney Graham conducted the peo
| pie's case and the defendant was repre
sented by Attorney D. A. Ryan of Gen
eral Barnes' office.
The witnesses examined were those who
gave their testimony at the Coroner's in
quest. They were Willie Walsh, Sam
Casel, W. M. Morris, Richard N. Nias.
C M. Foster. J. T. Baxter, George Bay
ford, Policeman C. M. Janes and Dr.
Gallagher, who made the autopsy on
Hllderbrand's body.
The evidence of the witnesses was upon
the shooting and the coolness and deliber
ation with which it was done. George
Bayford's testimony was the most dra
matic. He said that he heard the first
shot, and on looking out of the dining
room of the cafe he saw Rosser with the
revolver in his hand. Rosser took steady
alm and fired a second shot. He then
slowly walked about seven paces In the
direction of, the Stevenson-street en
trance and raising nls revolver aloft
yelled, "What's the matter with the Ten
nessee boys?" and ran into the street.
Policeman, Janes testified that when he
arrested Rwsser he asked him:
"What the matter, young fellow?"
Rosser replied:
"Do you want some of it? I shot a
man."
Janes asked him "what for" and he re
plied:
"None of your business."
After Janes took him back to the mar
ket to the scene of the shooting, Janes
said:
"Did you shoot that man?" and Rosser
replied:
"Yes; I shot him like a soldier."
Janes asked why he shot him and he
again repleld: None of your busi
ness." Then he grabbed Janes by the
throa.t and yelled, "Tennessee boys, where
are you?"
In cross examining the witness the at
torney for the defendant contented him
self by asking questions as to whether
there was any loud language or signs of
a dispute before the shooting, and in every
instance he was answered in the negative.
After the prosecution rested the de
fendant's attorney said he had no evi
dence to offer and the Judge held the de
fendant to answer before the Superior
Court on the charge of murder without
ball.
Asthma, bronchitis, catarrh cureguarant'd. Dr.
Gordin, Sanitarium, 514 Pine, nr. Kearny, S.F. Cal.

GEN. MERRIAM .
SAYS HE HEARD
NO COMPLAINTS
Troops at Honolulu in
Good Health.
DISEASE ON TRANSPORTS
NO HITCH IN THE TRANSFER OF
THE ISLANDS.
Will Require Years to Perfect Per
manent Military Quarters â No
Accommodations for Addi
tional Troops.
Major General H. C. Merriam arrivea
at an early hour yesterday morning on
the steamer Australia from Honolulu, and
Is happy to be back in his old quarters in
the Phelan building. Speaking of a re
ported misunderstanding a.s to the final
transfer of the islands to thÂ© representa
tives of the United States he said yes
terday in relating- his experiences that
there was no serious difficulty. A ques
tion was raised as to the proper method
of obtaining possession of the public prop
erty, but this was readily settled satisfac
torily to all parties concerned.
"It is a mistake," said the general, "to
say that there was even the slightest
friction in any quarter. On the contrary,
I found nothing but cordiality and good
â¢will on the part of the Hawaiian officials.
"They are now exercising their func
tions under the authority of the President
of the United States, and business is run
ning nmoothly."
With reference to the soliders now gar
risoned at Honolulu, General Men-lam
said that the camps were located on lanu
belonging largely to the Government, and
that little more can be done to improve
the temporary camps at present.
"Before I left for Honolulu," said the
fenerai, "I recommended the erection of
arracks for the housing of the troops,
but so far as I know nothing has been
done in that direction. If the material
and funds were at hand now the shelters
could be constructed before the rainy sea
eon sets in." He further stated that as
these things were not forthcoming at the
time of his departure, he had done the
best he could under the circumstances,
and ordered floors put In the tents. Asked
If the accommodations provided were suf
ficient he stated that if no more troops
were quartered there those now in camp
could be properly cared for.
"When it comes to the question of per
manent camps," said tne general, "it may
be a matter of years and will call for a
large expenditure of money before it is
settled. It is a proposition that must be
adjusted from a strategical standpoint
and when once settled should be per
manent. There is no dearth of proper lo
cations, but it will require much time to
secure and properly equip them for for
tifications and barracks. Harbor de
fense is the llrst lte-m to be considered
and Congress handles such matters. I
trust that such arrangements as it is
possible to make will be concluded before
the bad weather begins and that there
will be no serious trouble."
General Merriam's attention was called
to the fact that convalescent soldiers who
returned on the Australia complained
bitterly of their treatment on board the
transport and in camp at Honolulu.
"I have heard none of these com
plaints," said he, "and It has been ray
duty to not only hear grievances, but to
correct them. The accommodations in
camp were the best that could be afford
ed and the hardships were incident to the
heat, to which the men were not accus
tomed. While the temperature is not
high, it is depressing and very disagree
able to those unaccumated. I must con
fess that I am glad to get back to the
cool weather of San Francisco.
"As to the command stationed there,
the men are in exceptionally good health
and nearly all the sickness ti-.it has de
veloped was carried there on the trans
ports. Naturally there have been sev
eral deaths. I realize the discomforts
that must exist on transports in the
tropics and that all cannot have state
rooms. Most of the complaints. I think,
come from the fnct that nearly all the
volunteers in private life have been ac
customed to travel in good style and find
It hard to adjust themselves to the hard
ships incident to time of war."
When asked if he expected to go to
Manila. East or remain in San Francisco,
General Merrlam bluntly replied that he
expected to "obey orders" and did not
know what they would be. He further
stated that from his knowledge of the
â¢work on hand when he left the islands, the
Hawaiian Commissioners need not be ex
pected to return for two or three weeks.
AWAY FOR MANILA.
Troops Want General Miller to
Command the Expedition.
General Miller's recommendation that
Batteries A and D of the First Battalion
of California Heavy Artillery, under com
mand of Major Rice, be included In the
list of those troops to be sent to Manila,
was favorably acted upon by the War De
partment. The following dispatch was
received by the general late on Monday
night and was communicated by him the
first thing yesterday morning to Major
Bice:
WASHINGTON, D. C, Sept. 19. 189S
Brigadier General M. P. Miller. U. S. Volun
teers. Ran Francisco, Cal.: Your recommenda
tion that California Heavy Artillery, consisting
of commanding officer, adjutant and portion of
Batteries A and D, ffo to Manila, if? approved
by the Acting Secretary of War. They will
proceed to that point with the troops ordered
reporting upon arrival to the commanding gen
eral for duty. By command Major General
Miles. H. C. CORBIN,
Adjutant GeneraJ.
The Acting Secretary of War wired
Quartermaster Long yesterday directing
him that he furnish transports for 5000
men to go to Manila. He is instructed to
use the returning transports for this pur
pope. The Senator and China left Manila
August 24 and 30; Inoiana, September 1-
Pennsylvania, September 9; Newport'
September 13, and Zealandia. August 24 '
The War Department has directed that
only 5000 troops be sent to Manila but in
designating the forces that are to go
overlooked the fact that trans
ports will be required for considerably
over 6000 troops. It is now positively
known that the transports tuat have
Bailed heretofore have been fearfully
overcrowded, and It Is not improbable
that the City of Peking may be impressed
Into service, and perhaps another one of
the larger vessels that was used upon
some of the previous expeditions
The troops that are to leave want Gen
eral Merriam to command the expedition
The general himself is anxious to go'
but he refuses to use any influence to
have himself appointed. He believed
tnat he has merited the honor and that
It should come to him unsought. It Is not
probable that General Merriam will co
General Miller had a long conference
â with General Merriam yesterday morn-
Ing, but their Interview wa.s relative only
to the departure of the trooys. The first
of the forces should get away Inside of
two weeks, while the last of the forces
(should be well upon their way by the last
of next month.
The order for the departure of the
troops will require that the Second Bat
talion of the First Washington, doing
garrison duty at Angel Island, be re
lieved from duty there and moved to the
Presidio. They will he replaced by a de
tachment from Battery I of the" Third
Artillery.
Private Edward N. Montgomery, Bat
tery D. California Heavy Artillery, who
was tried by a general court-martial upon
the charge of being absent without leave
was found guilty and sentenced to be
dishonorably discharged from the ser
vice, forfeiting all allowances due him
and to be confined upon Aloatraz Island
at hard labor for three months.
Board of Health to Meet.
The Board of Health will meet this
evening at 8 o'clock at the City Hall to
discuss the management of sanitary mat
ters for the ensuing year under a reduced
appropriation of money.
TWO WOMEN
CALL HIM
HUSBAND
Mr. Nelson of Penryn
in a Dilemma.
AUSTRIAN AND ENGLISH WIVES
STRANGE CLAIM OF A NOBLE
VIENNESE LADY.
She Says He Is Rich, Tried to Pen
sion Her Off and Evaded the
Obligation â An Embroglio
in High Society.
William Henry Nelson of Penryn and
Loomis Is in the unpleasant dilemma of
having two wives.
Mrs. Nelson, the recognized wife who
Is well known and has been much ad
mired in the English colony at Penryn,
is one, and the other Is a lady who has
Just arrived from Vienna. The latter
brings papers and letters which leaves no
room for doubt that she was married
to Mr. Nelson In 1886 at the British Em
bassy in the embassy chapel by the Rev.
William H. Heckler and in the presence
of her father and friends.
She has asked and obtained the assist
ance of the British Consul and also of
the Austrian. Her people are influential
in Vienna and admitted to the royal
court, which is the most exclusive in Eu
rope, no one having less that sixteen no
ble ancestors being allowed at court. She
Is the daughter of Captain Smeschkall of
the Austrian army. Her brother is an
officer in the regular army and is at
present stationed in Vienna.
After their marriage the Nelsons pur
chased a home in Vienna. Mr. Nelson
had a large fortune and she lived in the
best style. They traveled three months
every year and went frequently to Mr.
Nelson's father's home in England. Mrs.
Nelson's younger sister was to be mar
ried, and with her husband she started
to attend the wedding.
"You go to your lather's and I will
stop and pick up your brother at his
quarters and we will come on together,"
said Mr. Nelson to his wife. There had
been no trouble of any kind and she
was much surprised when her brother
arrived alone. Mr. Nelson never did ar
rive.
After a long search by detectives the
missing husband was found in Penryn,
California.
Mrs. Nelson appealed to his people in
England, but though they could offer
lime advice they told her to come to
America. This was two years ago, and
she came.
The journey from London to Penryn,
Cal., is a long trip for a lady who is not
accustomed to go about alone.
Mrs. Nelson, as she calls herself, ar
rived at Pepryn in the nighttime. When
she found the station closed she said:
"It is very dark! How can 1 get oft
here alone?"
"The Postmaster will be here for the
mail bag," she was told, and later with
him she could drive but to her husband's
house. No one was up, but she called her
husband:
â¢Willie! Willie!"
When he asked who was making such a
row downstairs, she answered.
"It is I, your wife."
"But you cannot stay here; for God's
sake be quiet."
In the room lay the woman that in the
English settlement at Penryn was known
as Mrs. Nelson. She was very ill, and
quiet was a matter of life and death
with her.
"I will do anything if you will only
come away and not make a scandal," said
Mrs. Nelson, as she consented to acom
pany him. They drove to Sacramento,
which is the nearest town to Penryn.
On the way Mr. Nelson asked her to
drive, saying that he would get out and
walk. In telling the story of that mid
night drive, Mrs Nelson said:
"I heard his steps on the road behind
me, and I was afraid and stopped the
horses, .and said to him, 'You must get
in beside me.' "
It was a lonely spot on the road where,
he told her, a man had been murdered not
long before. Continuing she said in her
foreign way:
"I told him the consul expected me the
next day to call on him; he would look
for me if I did not appear on time, and
he knew where I had gone.
"When we arrived at Sacramento we
made an agreement. In my country there
is no divorce. He may go as he will. 1 do
not care for him to come back. But
shall I go back to my father's house a
burden to my father? 1 said no I would
"He promised to send me $15,000 as
soon as I was in Vienna, and be
sides a regular allowance. He gave me
then $3000 and bought my tickets back
to Austria. A friend of his stayed to see
me safely started and he went back to
his home, for he thought only of the sick
woman and the little baby.
"I waited and I waited and two years
have passed, but not one word or one dol
lar have 1 had from him, and so I have
come to America once more.
"Now I have engaged lawyers and will
see what the law can do. I shall not go
back so easily this time.
Mrs. Nelson is an Austrian and speaks
but broken English, but she intends to
have her rights if she can get them.
She has employed detectives both In Lon
don, where Mr. Nelson's securities are
held, and also in this country to find out
what his property, which is very large, is.
He owned a general store at Loomis, but
has put It out of his own hands and the
house at Penryn has been transferred to
the second Mrs. Nelson.
The latter is an English lady and much
liked and much admired in the English
colony. She came out from England with
Mr. Nelson and is a lady of quite as good
family as the Austrian wife. She has
TO OPEN WITH MASONIC RITES
Dedication of tKe WidoWs' and Or
phans' Jlome Set for October 13.
THE Masonic Home for Widows and Orphans which has been under
course of construction at Deooto will be completed by October 13
and on that day the building will be dedicated by the Masons and In
the presence of as many of that order as will come to the celebra
tion. It is the intention of the Masons to make the opening of the home
an event long to be remembered, and great preparations are' being made
to perfect a programme and to induce as many of the order as possible to
attend the ceremonies. At a recent meeting of the relief board of this city
a committee was appointed to take charge of any arrangements that might
be necessary to further the comfort and easy transportation of those Masons
who attended from this city and to stir up any latent enthusiasm that
might have escaped the occasion. William E. Lutz of Pacific Lodge is
chairman of the committee, and he is assisted by William McNair of Ex
celsior Lodge and G. C. Groezinger of Crockett Lodge.
The Masons have a ceremony of their own for the dedication of their
buildings, and this will be lollowed with all the solemnity and dignity
of the Masonic rites.
Charles L. Patton, chairman of the board of trustees of the institution
will open the proceedings by announcing the completion of the home and
then he will formally request the grand master, Thomas Flint and the
grand officers to dedicate the building to the noble charity for which it was
intended.
It is expected that at. least 10,000 Masons and their ends will' be pres

A BOTTLE AND A BULLET.
Lockie H. Josselyn, an Army Hospital Steward, Accused
of Attempting to Chloroform and Shoot His Wife.
I^ockie H. Josselyn, a member of the
division hospital corps at the Presidio.
was arrested Friday night on the charge
of discharging Urearms within the city
limits, but if the police can ilnd his wife
a more serious charge will be booked
against him. About 7 o'clock Friday night
a young woman came running out of the
lodging-house on the corner of Eddy and
Jones streets and told some policemen
that her husband, Lockie Josselyn, had
forced his way into her room and had
attempted to chloroform her. Failing in
that he pulled a revolver out of his pock
et and tried to shoot her. She broke
away from him and ran into the street
The policemen just then heard the report
of a revolver in a lane in the rear of the
lodging-house and learned that Josselyn

other wife has been a terrible blow to

Henry Nelson, of the Indian Civil Service,
and a brother of Major Horatio Nelson of
the Twenty-first Regiment In the English
army, who is the Governor of Her Maj
esty's prison at Redding, England, which
post he received as a mark of honor for

case against her husband, and say if she

TO GLEAN EVIDENCE
FOR MRS. BOTKIN
CHARACTER WITNESSES WILL
BE BROUGHT.
Attorneys Knight and Wheeler Leave
This Morning for Eureka on the
Pomona â Will Gather
Testimony.
The defenders of Mrs. Cordelia Botkln
are now going to take their turn at gath
ering evidence, and to this end Mr.
Knight and Mr. Wheeler will leave this
morning for Eureka on the steamer Po
mona.
The course of the prosecution has
greatly simplified the work that lies be
fore the defense. Chief Lees has already
called in his witnesses and has reduced
their actual evidence to affidavits, and
these he has made public through the
press.
While the mass of stuff that Lees has
given out made interesting reading it was
invaluable to the defense for that it
clearly outlined the facts and character
of evidence that they will be compelled
to combat should the case come up for
trial in this State.
Mr. Wheeler said yesterday: "Mr.
Knight and 1 expect to leave to-morrow
morning for Eureka, Mr. Knight to visit
his mother, and I to attend to some cases
that I have before the court there. We
will probably take advantage of our pres
ence in Humboldt to get hold of a numÂ«
ber of witnesses that we shall need to
testify to the former good character of
Mrs. Botkin wtien the case finally cornea
up for trial. Yes, we shall undoubtedly
introduce other than character witnesses,
but of the nature of their testimony I am'
of course, not at liberty to speak. Mr
Knight's stay In Humboldt will not be a
long one, but I shall not return until our
case comes up before Governor Budd
again, unless something unforseen should
arise."

"MARTHA" AT
THE TIVOLI
I dare say that most people have heard
"Martha" sung before. Those who have
not have probably read reviews upon it
or heard the favorite gems of this effer
vescent opera.
Miss Lichter, who so eminently distin
guished herself during this season, seems
to have acquired a yet more finished
grace of style during the last few weeks
and it would be difficult to conceive any
thing more complete than her rendering
of "Martha."
Miss Linck had a very suitable part and
impressed her admirers to another great
triumph. The high notes of her voice
are simply superb in their limpidity and
without apparent effort she achieves pro
digies of vocaltsm.
There is a kind of magical glamour
about Pruette's Plunket. He claims this
his favorite role, and does not say too
much. The subilety of lus baritone and
his entire work In this opera stamp It as
a masterpiece. Lovely music, no doubt,
and we have heard it for years without
getting too much of it.
Zerni's sinking is characterized by great
cleverness and charm of treatment. He
has all the resources of his talent at his
finger ends, but he is not feverishly anx
ious or petulant.
I must cay that the ensemble was mag
nificently harmonious during the entire
performance. Difficulties of execution
are surmounted with great ease, and
Misses Lichter and Linck and Messrs.
Pruette and Zerni are bright examples of
the grand style that oocur to me.
It is impossible to keep pace with all
the music which is rife at this season in
ban 1- rancisoo. It must be understood
that many detailed criticisms of the dif
ferent operas are omitted from this col
umn simply because of lack of space.
1 am, however, looking forward lor a
long and lusting musical treat until the
end ol the grand opera season at our
little jewel case," the Tivoli, and will
endeavor hereafter to kf>ep my friends,
tne true lovers of good music, posted on
the current events. G. H. MORRE

THREE FIGHTS
THAT PLEASED
THE AUDIENCE
Pugilist Lawlor Need
Not Go East:
RIB ROASTED BY MAXWELL
k
TWENTY BOUNDS OF BOXING
THE BOYS FANCIED.
An Evening's Fistic Entertainment
That Attracted a Large Gath- '
ering of Sports at Wood
ward's Pavilion. .â â¢'â¢â¢":
The newly organized Western Athletic
Club gave its first entertainment last
evening in Woodward's Pavilion, and it
proved a success in every particular.
Thanks to Captain James Gillen the ring
floor had been well padded, thereby pre
venting any possible danger to the con
testants who might have sustained a
heavy fall.
Jimmy Reilly won a decision over "Kid"
O'Brien, the slogger, and the referee,
"Jim" Kennedy, was hooted by the gal
lery sports, who proved that they did not
know the first thing about Queensberry
rules. Although wild and awkward In
his delivery, O'Brien fought gamely, but
Koilly stopped almost all- of the blows di
rected at his body with his glove, and the
crowd on high yelled with delight each
time they heard a sound from a smack
of gloves.
Chief Lees distinguished himself by or
dering the captain of the police to stop
the McCormick and "Young Jackson
fight In the ninth round, but it would be
difficult to imagine what prompted the
worthy Chief to issue such an ultimatum,
as neither of the "dubs" could inflict the
least punishment, although they may
have possibly succumbed from exhaus
tion.
The event of the evening was a twenty
round contest between Jimmy Lawlor, the
light-weight pugilist who recently jour
neyed to New York City expecting to get
a match with some of the Eastern cham
pions, and Sammy Maxwell of Los An
gelesâweight 132 pounds.
The betting was $10 to $6 In favor of
Lawlor, but considerable money from
the south was placed on Maxwell'by merÂ»
who had seen both pugilists perform.
"Jack" Stelznor was selected referee
of this contest, Lawlor was singularly
confident of polishing off his opponent in
quick order, but after he had a mix-up
in a few rounds with the sturdy lad from
j the orange groves he apparently wan
dered back to the old fireside and hoped
i for the best.
Maxwell, who Is a well proportioned
I fellow with good limbs and fine chest,
i is as quick as a cat on his pins and can
! deliver first-class goods at close range.
I He had Lawlor's ribs in trouble before
! the end of the third round, and all
through the mill In fact the San Fran
j ciscan's body was a target which stopped
several right hand gloves.
Lawlor some yÂ»ars ago carried a left
which went straight from the shoulder
and annoyed many a pugnacious face,
bat of late he has shifted In tactics and
now resorts to left and right swings in
the hope of landing a knockout, which
he has succeeded in accomplishing when
confronted by slow and reckless Doxers.
He found Maxwell altogether too shifty,
and try as best he could he failed at each
attempt to land his right on the jaw over
Maxwell's guard. Lawlor's left repeatedly
went over the shoulder or around the
neck of his opponent.
The contest, although it lasted twenty
rounds, can be told in few words. Max
well scored a long lead in points up to
the thirteenth round. Then Lawlor seem
ingly commenced to realize that he was
in the ring simply as a punching-bag for
hlfl opponent to practice on and he made
several desperate attempts to connect his
glove with Maxwell's Jaw, but failed re
peatedly.
It was a game very much one-sided up
to the sixteenth round, when Lawlor be
gan to encourage his friends by an occa
sional dash, but his fire was "invariably
returned with telling effect. There was
not a single round during the mill that
could be placed to the credit of the San
Franciscan, who was seemingly very
much chagrined when Referee Stelzner
awarded the contest to Maxwell.
The entertainment, which was unques
tionably good, pleased all present.
CANOEISTS RACED ON
OAKLAND CREEK
BEATRICE MADE THE FASTEST
TIME OVER THE COURSE.
A Series of Small Boat Contests Will
Be Held on the San Antonio
Estuary.
In 1593 there was organized the Oak
land Navy, with headquarters on San
Antonio Estuary, the only officials being
five committeemen, a secre.-.ry, measurer
and referee. Once a month from May to
December a regatta was held, the rules
for which were charmin~- in their sim
plicity. Boats of any size or rig might
enter, all started together, there were no
time allowances for difference in length
over all or on the water line, and the boat
that crossed the finishing line first won.
Sloops, cutters, yawls, schooners, canoes,
"Whitehalls with sails and craft of all
sizes entered the regattas, which afford
ed exciting and interesting sport. The
navy drew up a programme for 1897, but
during 1898 nothing has been heard of it.
With the view, therefore, of reviving
the interest in small boat sailing among
those who live on or near San Antonio
Estuary, the Oakland Canoe Club has
drawn up a programme of races. The
club has a* comfortable and convenient
boathouse on Sessions Basin, East Oak
land, and has on its roll the owners of
almost every kind of pleasure craft, from
a handsome schooner like La Paloma or a
fine sloop like Commodore Philip Caduc's
Annie, to a gasoline launch, a rowboat or
canoe.
The first of a series of races which the
club proposes to hold was sailed on Sun
day, September 18, on the estuary over a
triangular course at slack water and
with a strong westerly breeze. The fol
lowing boats entered. Sloops, Beatrice.
Ah Wanhee and Surprise; the canoes
Brownie, Codia. Jack. Nereid and Whine.
The sloop Beatrice made the best time over
the course. 1 hour 1 minute and 30 sec
onds; the sloop Ah Wanhee was second
and the canoe Jack third. The winner
was sailed by Charles Stewart, secretary
of the Oakland Canoe Club.
STABBED TWO MEN.
Andrew Borgen Attempts Murder in
a Quarrel Over a Woman.
In a quarrel over a woman Andrew
Borgen, a private ln Company M of the
Eighth California Volunteers, last even-
Ing cut Thomas Lawler of Battery A and
stabbed Benazie Matrla, a civilian living
at 625 Pacific street. The row started in
a saloon on that thoroughfare.
Borgen was ln the saloon drinking with
a woman and claims that Lawler struck
him in the face. He drew a razor and
slashed Lawler across the left elbow, in
flicting a deep wound. After the cutting
he dropped his weapon and started down
the street, pursued by a large crowd of
citizens. Matria caught up with Borgen
and when he attempted to stop him was
stabbed in the right temple with a pocket
knife. The blow which he received was
so powerful that when the knife struck
the skull the blade was nearly bent dou
ble.
The injured men wera taken to the Har
bor Hospital for treatment, and Borgen
was charged at the California street sta
tion with assault to commit murder.

THE LEASING OF THE
NORTH PACIFIC ROAD
THE CALIFORNIA NORTHWEST
ERN ASSUMES CHARGE.
No ChangfPfen the Policy or in
the Management of the
Railway Will
Result.
The San Francisco and North Pacific
Railway Company yesterday leased and
delivered all of its property to the Cali
fornia Northwestern Railway,. Company,
which corporation will operate the road.
The consideration is the net profits of
the road, and the lease runs for twenty
years.
According to the charter of the San
Francisco and North Pacific Company no
new connecting- lines can be built, so the
California Northwestern has been Incor
porated to build a line from the San
Francisco and North Pacific to the red
woods of Mendoclno County. Four points
for starting the new road have been con
sidered, but as yet none has been decided
upon.
Circulars were sent out yesterday to all
of the employes of the road notifying
them of the change, and advisilng them
that the policy would not be changed
from that In effect under the former
management.
The directors of the new corporation
are: G. Palache, president; G. W. Pres
cott, vice president: George A. Pope, F.
A. Wlckersham and J. A. Isham. The
following appointments have been made, ;
and the gentlemen entered upon their dv- i
ties yesterday: H t C. Whiting, general j
manager, San Francisco: F. K. Zook. su
perintendent and chief engineer. Tibu
ron; R. X. Ryan, general passenger and
freight agent, San Francisco; Thomas
Mellersh, secretary and controller, San
Francisco; George C. Hickox, cashier, San
Francisco; John Bonner, master mechan
ic, Tiburon; George H. McMullin, train
dispatcher, Tiburon; C. M. Anderson,
storekeeper. Tiburon.

COMBINE FOR PROGRESS.
The Polk-street merchants, who were
among the first to start an Improvement
club, have made another advance ana
formed a club which Is to be known as
, the "Polk-street Progress Club." This or
ganization will look out for all improve
ments necessary not only on Polk street
direct, but also on the adjoining streets
leading into that thoroughfare.
Unlike the majority of improvement
clubs throughout the city, the Polk-street
Progress Club will be conducted on the
same principles as is the Merchants' As
sociation. A board of directors will do
all the executive work necessary and the
members will not be called upon to lose
their time attending every- meeting. Tn<,
following have been elected officers and
directors for the first term: President,
Nathan Blbo; secretary, M. Wale; treas
urer, Val Schmidt; board of directorsâ L.
H. Kohn (chairman). Johnston Elliott. M
H. Shepard, W. B. Bradbury Vai
Schmidt, "W. McMann and Goldwln Mc-
Avoy.
School Board Meeting.
The School Board will meet this even
ing in committee of the whole to consider
bids for a site for the Mission school
building. An extra session of the board
will follow the committee meeting.
